       An Introduction to

 Investigative journalism is not a career path for the faint of heart.

the nearly 10 years since the first edition of Digging Deeper was publishe
rnalism in North America has experienced a seismic shift. The story is we
wn. Advertisers have fled print and broadcast outlets in search of cheap
 more targeted advertising online. Massive cuts in personnel and conte
e followed, and traditional news outlets have scrambled to find new ways
d the journalistic content that is so vital to a vigorously healthy democracy
nwhile, the rise of social media has created new opportunities for informa
 dissemination, and journalistic organizations no longer control the mean
istribution. Anyone with a computer or smartphone can publish breaki
s instantly, to a worldwide audience.
 Some argue that even while a single tweet may lack detail, or even be com
ely wrong, the greater engagement with the audience and the involvemen
the audience in the enterprise of news gathering itself is replacing “ol
ia with a new model. This participatory journalism, they argue, may n
ays get it right, but it corrects itself through the power of the “crowd.” O
s die. New ways replace them.
 Is there a reason to dig deeper anymore?
 In truth, the need to carefully gather and assess information is as great
y’s media environment as it ever was, maybe greater. The skills of diggi
per, of producing carefully researched investigative and enterprising jou
ism, are one way that those who seek to pursue journalism as more than
by can distinguish themselves from the crowd. Simply put, it’s better to
ight the first time.
 This is a book about getting it right, of using the many resources ava
 to journalists and anyone who wishes to practise journalism, to find wh
ous investigative reporter Carl Bernstein called “the best obtainable versi
he truth.”
 In these pages, you'll find advice on journalistic practice, a rich guide
   YY . Investigative Journalism

```

```
 per, and an introduction to data journalism, the fastest growing part of jour
 ism today. We'll show you where to look when the well seems dry, and ho
 write your story so you don't expose yourself to an expensive and damagin
 suit. Whether you are working on a large multimedia investigative packag
 simply trying to find a source for a story you'll publish or broadcast today
s book, and its online companion website, will help you find what you need.

hat Is Investigative Journalism?

e of the most useful definitions comes from the US organization Investigativ
 orters and Editors (IRE), which used a poll of its memes to Co cenine tha
 estigative journalism is “in-depth reporti
meone wants to keep secret, and is largely the reporter’s own work.” Later, th
 anization expanded the definition to include “uncovering systemic failure
 misguided policies.”
 For this book, we broadened the definition further to include short-term
 estigative work done by daily journalists, bloggers and serious citizen jour
 sts, and investigations by non-journalists. However, the core principle is th
 e for all: investigators seek the truth.’By that, we mean the facts, as best a
 y can be determined, laid out fairly and comprehensively.
 At their best, investigative stories reveal injustice and misery, expose
 ngdoing, force accountability, uncover wasteful spending, and point out th
lures of public officials or public systems. Ideally, the stories motivate peopl
authority to fix the problems.
 Investigative journalists play a critical role in our democratic society
 ing as a counterweight to the many forces that seek to control the flow o
 ormation and obscure the truth.

here Daily Journalism Ends
nd the Investigation Begins

picious about him driving a van with three passengers. A struggle ensued
 ween the two men, and Richards was charged with resisting arrest and tryin
choke the officer. He was convicted in the lower courts, and he appealed.
 During the 1999 appeal, he raised [the ] [spectre ] [of ][what ] [has ] [become ] [known]

 After a flurry of coverage in the Toronto newspapers, the Richards st
d. The appeal court later quashed his conviction, although for reasons
 ectly connected to his claim of racial profiling.’
 The coverage of the Richards case was typical of daily news reporting
 h contentious issues: honest accounts of what two opposing sides said

 t took place in a public venue (in this case, the courts). This kind of rout
 rnalism is very important. It constitutes most of the news coverage you
 daily and weekly newspapers, on television and radio, and on the Internet
 ves public debate on a day-to-day basis, contributes to our understanding
mplex issues, and routinely delivers information that would otherwise h
 n kept secret by businesses, governments, and others who benefit fro
 k of public discourse. It is the life's work of thousands of journalists. B
 any general assignment or beat reporter knows, it has limitations. Pee

 e is a new development. So, no matter how good the journalist, the finished
duct may be just one piece of the bigger story.
 One consequence of so many early deadlines is that the news cycle often
ecomethe s pin cycle. Armies of media-relations officers are deployed by

ernments, corporations, and interest groups. These professional informa managers offer invaluable help to reporters who need a quick comment,
 tion, or fact, but these people also act as a filter, ensuring that the carefully
 ted message of their employers gets through to the public. Access to key
 yers is restricted, and the media-relations people become conduits for whatr information is to be released. Ih some cases, as is becoming common a
 federal government level in Canada, even the media-relations people no
 er do interviews. Reporters are provided with emailed responses that may
 even answer the questions the reporters posed in the first place. And the
 nalists have little choice but to co-operate because they need something,
nything, by their deadline. .
 It’s not hard to understand why these things have happened. The media tions community has made a careful study of the medias methods and
ws how to exploit weaknesses. In his book, In The News: The Practice o
ia Relations in Canada, William Wray Carney devotes an entire chapter to
 mechanics of the news cycle and succinctly captures the challenges faced by
 nalists at daily news outlets:

 Generally speaking, day-to-day reporters are rushed, inexpert, trying
 to get to the heart of the matter as quickly as possible and working
 under career-ending deadline pressure. . . . The journalist relies on the,
 communicator for basic information and access to expert authorities,
 while the communicator relies on the journalistyto disseminate infor mation and reach key audiences.’

 symbiotic relationship is often seen in business reporting, because
panies that trade on public stock exchanges are required to release any
 rmation that may be material to the stock price. For example, on 6 January
 the airlines WestJet and Air Canada issued news releases reporting pas er numbers for December 2013 as compared to December 2012. Each
  release played up positive numbers and provided quotations from their
 ective presidents.
 The releases became the basis for stories across traditional media and on

s, and the news was widely tweeted, with the traditional news stories writfrom the press releases often the source for the tweets. The story was picked
by the Canadian Press news service [and ] [many ] [newspapers ] [across ] [Canada.]

 Manipulation of the daily media goes well beyond the relatively sim
chanism of the handout. Journalists conducting interviews often encount
ple who have been specially schooled in how to disarm and use membe
the media to deliver a canned message to the public. Former CBS execut
ducer cum media trainer George Merlis counsels those facing intervie
craft “intentional message statements,’ which can be deftly dropped i

ponses to reporters’ questions.*
 “T tell my clients to never go into an interview without at least five I
tentional message statements],” he writes. Later, he explains how an int
wee can easily shift from the reporter’s question to an IMS by paying “fact
 service” to the question, then using a verbal bridge to move on to the p
ed message.’
 Canadian media trainer Barry McLoughlin also counsels clients to use
dging technique before “driv[ing] your message back onto your messa
ck” His firm, McLoughlin Media, suggests using the McLoughlin Wedge
echnique to insert a pre-planned message into an interview in short chunk
inning with an opening statement. “Front-end-load your message,’
 ’s Encountering the Media guide counsels, “so if you get interrupted, you
eady got your message out.”

 In the fall of 2002, Jim Rankin of the Toronto Star began a series of inves
ive projects that would reveal in unprecedented ways how the Toronto pol
eract with members of racial minorities. The most explosive of these came
2, when Rankin and a team of Star journalists published “Race and Crime
ch focused on racial profiling.
 Unlike the “here today, gone tomorrow’ stories on Clifton Richards seve
rs earlier, the stories had immediate impact. The advance of time and
owing sensitivity to minority concerns was certainly a factor in that, but mo
ortant was the tremendous depth of the Star series. The reporters analy
 Toronto Police Service's own electronic database of arrests, and made
mpelling case for the allegation that the police were pulling over black peo
re often than people of other colours for certain motor vehicle offences, s
driving without registration papers, that could not have been observed f

 The Star series rocked the city. Even though the police and the police unio
ied the allegations as forcefully as ever, this time the newspaper offere
pelling evidence, sparking a thorough public debate. It displayed the powe
investigative journalism to foster change. More than seven years after th
ginal series, Rankin and the Star published a February 2010 follow-up base
 additional data obtained through a freedom-of-information request tha
und up requiring seven years of appeals, court cases, and intense negotia
ns. But while the police fought release of the data, once the story came ou
t officers stopped and questioned black people three times as often as whites
 police chief—not the same chief as in 2002—didn‘t dispute the findings, an
sat down with the Star to explore the problem and search for solutions.’
 Rankin followed up yet again in 2012 and once more in the fall of 201
h another look at the issue, based on police stop data updated to the end
2. In conjunction with other Star journalists, Rankin and database specialis
drew Bailey conducted statistical analyses that were able to identify officer
o were stopping and questioning racial minorities at a greater rate tha
ir peers. Again, the reaction was more muted than in 2002 and the polic
icated that they were on the cusp of adopting similar statistical approache
help identify anomalies in their dealings with racial minorities.
 In effect, after a little more than a decade of determined reporting, th
ice had gone from condemning Rankin to adopting his methods.

 “This body of work is the thing I am proudest of in my career,” Rankin
 said in an interview. “We're probably most proud of the evolving
 discussion on this subject. It began, after our first series, with police
 denials, hostility, a boycott of the paper and an unsuccessful $2.7 bil lion class action lawsuit. Skip ahead to 2014. ... People are talking ina
 constructive way about a very real problem that affects the public trust
 of police and the safety of the city. Even better, but harder to gauge,
 is the impact this work has had on police services across the country.
 People are watching and learning.”

e Twelve Keys

riosity
iosity may have killed the cat, but it has given life to many an investigati
 y. It gives rise to the most important questions: I wonder what happene
onder why? Who was responsible? How much money was involved? Wh
 pens next? How is it supposed to work? How does it really work?
 The investigative journalist must viscerally want the answers to questio
 h as these. Curiosity is the engine that powers many other crucial par
the investigation. It also keeps you going when obstacles and frustratio
 ede you along the way.

s one can't be overemphasized. Being prepared will always, always pay
 the investigative reporter. Consider interviews. In daily journalism, th
 often conducted on the fly with little time to get to know much about
 erviewee, or sometimes even the subject. Investigative interviews, howeve
 conducted only after careful preparation. You will want to research pr
ctive interviewees, find out not only what they do and what they shou
 w, but also what they are like and how they may react in an interview. Y
 will examine documents, notes from previous interviews, and electron
, drawing up a list of questions or talking points before you come close

 ng an interview. Preparation is the = to other a of sn muPiEaL
 cess as well, especially writing. You will spend at least’ as much time
 material as writing the story.,

 re are probably as many systems for staying organized as there are investi
 reporters. The trick is to find one that works for you.
 Cecil Rosner, a Winnipeg-based journalist with CBC Television, learn
 lesson when he interviewed a heart researcher who was involved i
fessional dispute. “Every time [the researcher] spoke with someone, th
 ld be a separate entry, dated and timed, in his binder, explains Rosner.
 all there. And it allowed him to prove a case forcefully.”
 Rosner went on to co-author a book on David Milgaard, who spent

```

```
 he was innocent, and Rosner’s book When Justice Fails helped prove i
le writing the Milgaard book, Rosner applied what he had learned from
 heart researcher: “I organized material in file folders and binders, each
oted to a separate topic or chapter in Milgaard’s life. All the material within
h binder was chronologically arranged.” Rosner swears by the chronology
 way to make sense of material—one of the biggest challenges faced b
stigative writers.
 On any project, more and more information accumulates as the researcher
 deeper into the subject. There will be interview notes and transcripts, elec
ic files, links to websites and printouts, notations, databases and spreadsheet
, and any number of other paper documents. You'll know what each one i
ended for when you obtain it, but weeks or months later, you may become
rwhelmed by it all. Some reporters use manila file folders to stay organized,
rs keep spreadsheets, and still others use small file cards. Some make “t
 and “to interview” lists; others keep all of the phone numbers they come
ss on a single sheet or in a single file. The key is to be able to quickly fin
 document or interview you need and understand its significance, as well a
ome to understand the bigger picture and develop a list of key points. If you
 organized throughout, you will save hours, even days, of work when you
h the writing stage of your project.

ience,
akes patience to research and write an investigative story. You,are alway
aiting for something, whether it be a reluctant source to return your calls
ic officials to respond to freedom-of-information requests, or inspiration
it.
 For those accustomed to the faster pace of daily reporting, all of this waitcan be frustrating. But it is often the difference between getting most of th
y and getting all of iti Some projects can take months to go from idea t
ication or broadcast, and you will face many pressures to speed that process
Of course, you need to know when you have done enough research, when
 last document isn’t worth waiting for. But rushing can lead to incomplete,
 inaccurate, stories that will fade away as quickly as yesterday's headlines.

Tenacity .
 is one of the most important qualities for the investigative journalist.
y times, you will face obstacles that will tempt you to give up or giv

ces will be difficult to find [or ] [reluctant ] [to ] [speak ][to ][you. ] [Databases ] [will ] [seem]
enetrable and duointelieible.) Sometimes, you just won't have a clue how t

 Tenacity paid off for Lindsay Kines, the Vancouver Sun police reporter w
t told the stories of women who had vanished from the city’s Downtow
tside. The drug users and prostitutes were part of society's lowest stratu
ing in what is perceived to be, by far, Canada’s poorest and most dangero
ighbourhood. The Downtown Eastside comprises a few bleak blocks of ru
wn hotels, unsavoury characters, and a massive drug trade. Kines start
h a single story in 1997 about a woman looking for her missing sister. Ov
e, more cases came to light, and Kines stuck with the story, developi
tacts on the street and trust within the police department.
 He faced resistance in his own newsroom, and other media outlets most
ored the story. But, in time, Kines would help uncover what became
gest serial murder case in Canadian history. His work, and that of fell
orters Kim Bolan and Lori Culbert, helped reveal not only the extent of
ughter, but also the sorely deficient initial investigation by the Vancouv
ice. “The longer I stayed on the story, the more people felt comfortable wi
 and trusted me,” Kines said. “In the end, it helped me get crucial intervie
h disgruntled cops who had worked on the investigation. They trusted.
ause they said my reporting on the case had been fair, and when it ca
e to blow the lid off all the problems with the initial investigation, th
sted me to tell it”!
 Kines and Bolan went on to break the story of Robert William Pickton,
t Coquitlam pig farmer who was later convicted of second-degree murd
the deaths of six women: Sereena Abotsway, Mona Wilson, Andrea Joesbur
rnie Frey, Brenda Wolfe, and Georgina Papin. The two journalists won
ional Newspaper Award for that story, and, along with Culbert, were ru
s up for the Michener Award for their series.

s of a person's political contributions or property-tax assessments can rev

ch with the person you really want to talk to, and so on. The creative use o
 available resources, which is the subject of much of this book, can help you
 ssemble your information successfully.

esti reporting differs from the daily variety in that you have the time,
 the obligation, to be thorough and complete. While a daily story remains
 tly focused, an investigation follows up numerous leads and goes down
 y roads, not all of them productive. You want to know that you have
austed, or very nearly exhausted, the possibilities before you state something
 e true. The need to be thorough applies to every aspect of an investigation.
en you are engaged in the early stages and are laying the groundwork for
e detailed work later, you want to thoroughly canvass existing information
 a subject. This will help you thoroughly plan your research. Once into the
earch stage, you must thoroughly investigate leads. You should thoroughly
pare to write, and the writing itself needs to be thorough and complete. The
 lt is authoritative and has the potential to make a real difference.
 “The need to file for deadline should never override the need to do exhaust research,” says Rosner, regarding his Milgaard book. “But ata certain point
 journalist must ask: has enough work been done to validate my original
othesis? If the answer [is ][yes, ][then ] [the ][story ][can ] [be ][written ][or ][broadcast.”"’ ]

t, so you don’t want to add to your anxiety.

ement that comes his or her way. Facts stated in press releases, for example,

ressing the 11 key elements mentioned above is the one that makes them al

 the luxury of time that allows the extensive research, background checking,

g the investigative story alive.
 Of course, this doesn't mean that you can't do investigative journalism i
 are also doing daily assignments. You will simply have to fit the work in
ween other responsibilities. This is not impossible, but requires organization

u ll be amazed how much work you can get done in the time you didn't know

nclusion

y investigative journalists are motivated by something—a sense of outrage
uriosity, for example—that prompts them to continually ask deeper ques. When attempting to peel back the layers that separate truth from folly
vey Cashore, an award-winning CBC journalist and author, likes to ask
question, “How did we get here?”!? More often than not, such a question
 be answered only after rigorous research, which requires time, discipline,
 a zeal to uncover details that will prompt citizens to demand action. As
, an investigative journalist’s quest for truth has a moral dimension. For
 book Custodians of Conscience: Investigative Journalism and Public Virtue,
s Ettema and Theodore Glasser interviewed some of the top journalists in
US about their work, and this topic of morality came up:

 Their particular sort of reporting yields stories that are carefully veri fied and skillfully narrated accounts of specific injury and injustice but
 stories with a meaning that transcends the facts of the particular case.
 Their stories call attention to the breakdown of social systems and
 merit.”? A skeptic doesn’t dismiss merit, but instead tests every fact o

hat Makes a Good Investigative Story?

estigative stories can be on almost any topic imaginable and may take man
 roaches. They can be multi-part series in major metropolitan newspapers
ealing unsafe products or secret political deals. They can be segments
evision shows, such as the CBC’s the fifth estate. They can be one-off websit
 bing deeply into a problem or issue. They can be well-researched stories
 daily section of the newspaper or in TV newscasts, stories that go beyon
 daily handout cycle.
 All this said, some stories work better than others. There are certain qua
 s that are more likely to lead to a successful investigation.

e under-reported story

en The Hamilton Spectator investigated the ongoing problem of deliberate
 car fires in the vast rural reaches of the Southern Ontario city, one of t
ngs that made the story compelling was that it was so under-reported. Ev
 ugh the fires happened month after month and the calls often crackled acro
 emergency scanners in the newsroom, the Spectator had written almost not
 about the subject. No special-interest groups had taken up the case, and t
ic was virtually ignored by the police and fire departments, despite the f
 car fires are dangerous for those who fight them and amounted to a thi
 all fire calls in Hamilton in 2002 and 2003. Each fire was so small, it didn
 act much attention. But, put together, property losses from all of the fires we
 ressive—about $2 million each year. It was the classic under-reported story

 injustice

 estigations that uncover real injustice will always be well read. The reas
 David Milgaard story, discussed in the previous chapter, was such compe
g reading is because what happened to this young man was so unjust. Not o
 his teens when he was sent to jail, Milgaard was imprisoned for more th
 decades before the calm determination of his mother prompted anoth
 k at the case—and her son's eventual exoneration by way of DNA evidence

rruption or crime

 ruption and/or crime will always make for good investigations, althou
 y will also be some of the most difficult. The stories offer compelling readi
 have great potential to make a difference. The Vancouver Police might ha

```

```
 work of Lindsay Kines and his colleagues, but it would have taken them
h longer.

 aulty system or process

n Toronto Star reporter Robert Cribb fell ill after eating in one of tha
 s restaurants, he set out to investigate the state of Toronto's eateries an
 food-handling practices that take place out of sight in the kitchen. After
 battle to obtain a city food inspection database, Cribb wrote a blockbuster
 s of stories revealing not only the poor conditions in many of the city’
aurants, but showing that city inspectors, who were supposed to be keeping
food safe, were failing to do the required checks.‘ It was a classic story about
 stem that was supposed to work one way, but really worked another. The
 es prompted the city to crack down on bad eateries and introduce a posting
 em to alert diners to problems found by inspectors.

human element

 best stories, including the best investigative stories, have people at thei
 Whether the people are those affected by a government process gone
 g, or they are characters central to the story, without a human element
ody will read your ambitious six-part series.

mething new

 investigative story worthy of the term puts something on the record
 wasn't known publicly before. This can take the form of something com ly unknown (such as a public figure’s involvement in a financial scandal);
 thing widely believed but never proven (e.g., revelations about dirty res
 nts); or a previously unrecognized pattern, perhaps drawn from disparate
ic sources. For example, in 2012, the Toronto Star searched through court
 ments across Canada to find examples of when police lied in court, buildthe foundation for a story that won a National Newspaper Award.°

tory appropriate for your audience

matter how good your research, how extensive your interviews, how bril
 your synthesis of the facts, all that work is wasted if nobody watches,
ns to, or reads your story. This isn’t to say that your audience has to b
ersal—far from it. But you do need to report for the audience that will se

lication, such as a metropolitan daily newspaper, you may not want
nch into an investigation of the mating habits of chimpanzees. However
h a story could be perfectly appropriate for a wildlife magazine.

here Good Ideas Come From

re is no magic to finding ideas. They can come from just about anywhere
, some sources are more fruitful than others.

e beat

 ou cover a beat, you are already connected to one of the best sources f
 stigative story ideas. Beat reporters tend to develop significant experti
 their assigned areas and develop numerous sources. Inevitably, they wi
 e across stories that merit deeper investigation. If you are not on a be
 self, talk to other reporters who are. They may offer their ideas, or he
 develop your own. The Vancouver Sun’s missing-women investigation i
fect example of a story that began on the beat.

ippings

 ly news stories are often a fruitful source. Sometimes a brief clip will hi
 much bigger story that hasn't received much attention. A story about
 ster may prompt you to begin an investigation of the circumstances th
 e the disaster possible. On other occasions, an investigation begins becau
eporter wants to follow up on promises of action made by officials after pr
us stories on a subject. The Hamilton Spectator’s investigation into Ontario
 ve Clean program® began with brief news stories about alleged unfairne
 ineptitude in the mandatory emissions testing. That prompted the news
 er to file a freedom-of-information request for data, which the provinci
 ernment didn’t release for three years (and then only when ordered to
 This eventually resulted in stories that uncovered fraud at testing sites a
 wed how the science behind claims of air-quality improvements was unrel
 . The government moved quickly to make changes to the program.
 As you develop as an investigator, you will start to notice stories where y
 w there is more to be told. It is a good idea to keep a file of these clippings

sters

etimes, a story will land in your lap by way of a phone call, email, or letter.
t of other people out there are frustrated that something they feel is importis not getting the coverage it deserves. Much of the time, these calls will not

ediately dismiss people who seem to be obsessed with a perceived wrongg. Sometimes, these people are right. Consider the excitable Winnipeg war
ran who kept calling the CBC in the mid-1990s to talk about the chemical
are experiments he had undergone at Camp Suffield. That tip led to a CBC
itoba investigation that revealed the harrowing personal stories of veterans
 had been used as guinea pigs in Second World War experiments with
tering mustard gas.’ As part of the probe, the CBC obtained long-classified
uments from the Department of National Defence, including photographs,
raying the tests in stomach-turning detail. The surviving veterans were
tually compensated by the federal government.
 The key to assessing tips is to check them out. Question the tipster to see i
story makes sense and is internally consistent. Once off the phone, do some
c online research. If the tip still seems credible, start digging deeper.

eviewing and Auditing the Idea

great many factors have to be considered before you devote substantial
rgy and time to an investigation.
 Think about the final product, as much as you can imagine it at this stage
 you think it is something that will be of interest to your readers, listeners, o
wers? Is it the kind of story your news outlet would tell? What impact might
 story have? Is there an injustice to correct or a faulty system that could b
 nged? Is there a strong people element?
 \sI le feckal eid wae
      batond ; it2

 Ideally, can you reduce your inquiry to a simple question, such as, “How
ly people are dying from this drug?” or “Why isn’t this program working,
 what must be done to fix it?”
 Finally, for better or worse, can you bring your editors or producers on
rd? It doesn't matter how good the idea is if you don't have support from
r managers. You must make the case to them.
 If the answers to these kinds of questions are promising, you are ready t
 e to the next stage: developing a plan. Remember, this plan is not static
 a living document that you will amend as new information comes to light
 ember also that while we present these steps in an order here, not every
 stigation follows the same path. You are very likely, however, to include
t of these steps at some point in the project.

  d journalists always try as hard to disprove their hypothesis as to prove it.)
  In 2012, students in the investigative journalism workshop at the Universit
  ing’s College set out to investigate a cash-for-residency immigration schem
  ated by the Prince Edward Island (PEI) government. Through the “immi
  t partner program,’ a foreign national could obtain Canadian residency
  sting $200,000 in a PEI business and becoming a partner in its operation.
  A great deal had been written about the program in PEI media, but t
  s had largely been on involvement in the program by officials connecte
  he Liberal government. Less had been written on the business relationshi
  selves. After doing initial research, the students hypothesized that t
  tnerships” were mostly on paper. Their subsequent research was focused
  ing this hypothesis to determine if it were true.
  In doing so, researchers typically work from the general to the specifi
 m those most peripherally involved to those at the centre of the investigatio

 « From reading a great many previously published news stories, mostly

 * Because the PEI program stipulated that immigrant partners be either

 « After manually downloading thousands of corporate profile reports

  Immigrants further confirmed that few stayed on the Island.

  change regulations to outlaw the scheme.

  agency that ran the program.

resulting story revealed the inner workings of the program in a detail never

  P.E.I’s population by establishing new immigrant communities.
    But an investigation by journalism students at the University of
 King’s College has found that a decade after it started, the now-defunct

    For some, it became a way for foreign nationals to purchase entry
  into Canada, by making “investments” they would never recoup, in

  are now long gone from P.E.I. For businesses, it was an easy source of

pecial Situations

utting your losses

 matter how hard the journalist tries, some investigations are doomed

```

```
s that at first seemed promising fail to pan out. There may be a lack of th
 evidence needed to back up allegations or assertions. The people needed
erify the story may refuse to co-operate. Crucial information may not b
ssible through public records or by way of a leak from a friendly source.
le it is hard to admit defeat, you must recognize that in some cases, furthe
stigation is futile. Sometimes, the reason is simple: there was no story t
 in the first place. But this does not mean your efforts have been wasted.
y investigation introduces you to new sources of information or new ways
se those consulted in the past. What you amassed before the project was
ndoned may be of use in a future investigation. At the very least, you wil
 a better sense of when to cut your losses and move on to more promising
stigations.

veillance and undercover techniques

nalists strive to gather information in an open, transparent manner. It
dard practice for reporters to identify themselves as media representatives
n they speak to potential sources. They keep their voice recorders and cam in clear view, making sure that the subject knows his or her words o
ons are being recorded and may be publicized. But some stories cannot b
fied using conventional research methods, such as when no documents exis
 sources refuse to provide the evidence.

Going undercover demands careful strategy, specialized technology, and
rong ethical compass. Most ethical codes for journalists recognize tha
public interest may justify using covert means in rare circumstances.Th

            t activity i

   Is going undercover the only way to get the information?

 “Only after other efforts to obtain witnesses and documents have fail
uld a journalist consider using deception. As Nick Russell puts it in Moral
d the Media: Ethics in Canadian Journalism, “The journalist must first exhau
 traditional, socially acceptable methods of investigation before resorti
the clandestine””* Making up a false name, lying about credentials, secret
ording what people say or do—such conduct has the potential to undermin
lic trust in the journalist or the media as a whole. When journalists misrepre
t themselves while gathering information, their stories should explain t
thods used and why they were considered necessary. By disclosing, journalis
ow readers, listeners, or viewers to judge whether the ends justified the means
 There are also legal and privacy considerations when working undercover

e consent of at least one of those people. It is a criminal offence, for exampl
 publish or broadcast information picked up by a tape recorder secret
 running in a room where a private meeting is taking place.'” Journalis
anning to make secret recordings should consult with an editor or the ne
anization's legal advisors before proceeding.
 Having decided to conceal your identity, you must consider a number
istical issues. Beyond the ethical pitfalls, going undercover can be both dif
t and even dangerous.

 First off, never go it alone. If you're entering into a situation undercove
ruit a partner and rehearse your cover story until you get it straight—yo
mes and relationship to each other, where you live, your phone numbe
y you are there, and how you heard about the location. Two sets of eyes a

k to more people, gather more documents or observations, and increas

witness if the resulting story leads to litigation in which reporters are calle

ing into potentially dangerous situations comes with a high degree o
dictability. A partner provides a measure of protection in case your cove

atening. Have a plan to make a hasty exit in case things go awry or th
ct of the story becomes suspicious.
Another major consideration is equipment. There are several ways t

Going undercover can open up new storytelling possibilities for jour
s. A Toronto Star investigation into fraudulent telemarketing operations
 on hidden audio and video devices to document false claims being made
 goods sold to unsuspecting US citizens. The newspaper discovered tha
to was a haven for companies that were using the border as protection
st tougher US anti-fraud laws. In Canada, they were safely and cheapl
ting a massive US market while staying clear of the tougher laws stateside.

ployment publications. They showed up separately at the so-called boi

  Original evidence gathered this way can often prompt police investigation

nonymous threats of violence against the reporters; on the other hand, stor
 e this often generate huge public response, vigorous debate, and meaningf
blic-policy changes. Going undercover can be messy, time consuming, a
sky. But used carefully, it can also be powerful.

roject, Student Team, University

udents at the Halifax university spent six weeks investigating an immigr
on program in Prince Edward Island that had allowed immigrants to inv

 “Cashing In” was published on May 9, 2013, in The Huffington Post Cana

  The Canadian Press’s Halifax bureau. Tutton had done a number of ne
  stories about Prince Edward Island’s controversial provincial nominee p
 gram, but felt there was more to be told.
 What initial research did you do to confirm that the story was valid?
 We used the Factiva database to research previous news stories on
  subject, including work done by The Guardian in Charlottetown, and
 searched the PEI government's website for archival material.
 What was your initial theory as to what the story would be?
 We hypothesized that PEI effectively sold access to Canadian citizenship
  a way of raising money for Island businesses.
 Once you had confirmed that you had a story, how did you plan out y
  research?

  research goals. One set out to uncover how the program worked in
  context of Citizenship and Immigration Canada’s rules and regulatio
 another scoured Hansard and the Canada Gazette records for speci
  detail, the third began looking for immigrants who had participated,
  the fourth was on the hunt for businesses that had been part of the p
  gram. Fortuitously, a member of the group spoke Mandarin, enabling
  to communicate directly with immigrants who entered Canada thro

       Building a Foundation

        Gathering Information Already
        Published or Broadcast

  Locating and reviewing background information is an essential step
  in any investigation.

here is no single starting point that works for all investigations. Every stor
roblem is different and each investigation leads to its own array of human sour
line resources, and paper records. You may go directly to court records in se
f files involving the target of the investigation, or immediately pick up the ph
 find out what trusted contacts already know about the matter. But the m
gical and the least time-consuming starting point is often a review of mate
lready published about the person or subject, the secondary sources. Locating
eviewing background information is an essential step in any investigation. “
al,” says author Jessica Mitford, whose books have probed the funeral indu
nd the treatment of inmates in US prisons, among other issues, “is to know
ssible, more about your subject than the target of the investigation does.”!
  Media reports can be retrieved easily using online databases or libraries;
query on Google, Bing, or another Internet search engine will turn up m
ews stories if an issue has received significant attention. Media coverage prov
fast and usually reliable source of background information, while alerting yo
hat has already been reported. This is particularly helpful in working out whet
tip or disclosure is, in fact, news. If it turns out that the issue or controversy
lready received media attention, you may find there are still fresh angles t
xplored. Best of all, previous coverage offers information that has been packa
r a general audience and is not weighted down with jargon or technical de
hink of media stories as briefing notes that help you grasp the complexities

ubject as you dig deeper into the story and prepare to interview experts.
  The job may encompass a couple of hours of Internet searches, or day
earching through research databases or written material in a library, but
oal is always the same: to learn what has already been said so you don't hav
investigate” what is already known.
  Here are some secondary sources you might consult, with tips on ho
se them.

```

```
ditional Sources

spapers

ly a topic exists that hasn't been written about in a newspaper, so archive
 s are a logical place to start an investigation.
Newspapers famously provide the “first draft of history.’ Created on t
by harried reporters and editors, they are a basic record of the public li
public conversation of our society. These days, archives of many of t
st newspapers can be accessed online with services such as LexisNexis
a, and Factiva. These can be quite costly, but your local public or univer
ibrary may provide free access, often via an online signup.
Smaller papers, especially weeklies, are less likely to be archived this wa
ou may sometimes need to consult them. Many publish online editions,
 stories will show up through a general Google search. The early days of
essperson’s career may have been chronicled only in a weekly local paper
metown paper may carry a politician's damaging comments that were neve
ed to the big dailies. You may even need to find something obscure, such
ealing advertisement or notice.
When The Hamilton Spectator was investigating food poisonings in tha
in 2001, documents obtained under a freedom-of-information (FOI
st made reference to a wedding at which the bride had become ill, alon
 dozens of guests. Names were removed, as the law requires, but the rec

included the date of the banquet and a reference to the town of Grimsby
io. Taking those two pieces of information, the reporter looked throug
r’s worth of issues of the local weekly paper on microfilm at a librar
imsby. He eventually found a wedding announcement for a ceremon
hing the date of the banquet, tracked down the couple, and confirmed tha
d identified the correct wedding.
As the Spectator reporter did, you may need to consult small papers di
 on microfilm or in hard copy. Some papers will sell you back issues b
 These small papers are unlikely to be indexed, so you will need to kno
 your subject may have been in the news and then search manually. Mor
more small papers have online editions, however, and these sometimes
e free or paid archives.

azines

ines are published on just about any topic imaginable, and tend to prin
ial that is of greater depth and research than that found in newspapers

```

```
e. Don't forget to look for regional magazines, too, which often run piec
 local high-profile people.
 Finding articles is infinitely easier if you use an index. Traditionally, th
e bound, annual volumes, but now they have migrated online and can
essed through academic and public libraries. CPI.Q, the online version of
nadian Periodical Index, indexes more than 1.5 million articles, many fr
nadian publications. Many public libraries provide free access to patrons.
 articles, The Readers’ Guide to Periodical Literature is the standard hard-co
ource and is also available online through many libraries with full-text articl
 Many libraries will have the print versions of these indexes if they
l issued that way, though there has been a definite trend toward electron
dings, partly because they are cheaper. Print versions can prove invaluab
cause they bring together a large number of publications in one place a
 standardized subject headings, which can make searches more precise th
ing keywords.

urnals and other specialty publications

e step up the reliability ladder from magazines are academic journals,
ich researchers at universities, at other institutions, and in companies a
vernment showcase their latest work. Prestigious journals, such as the N

gland Journal of Medicine or the Canadian Medical Association Journa
equently create breaking news. Increasingly, they are putting their most new
rthy studies online, well before the hard copy of the edition is released. So
o have their own alert systems, which send you tables of contents days befo
 issue is published.
 There are hundreds and hundreds of other journals, many of which
ilable online. Specialized indexes, such as JSTOR, simplify searching a
ovide access to full-text articles. Check your local university library to se
u can get access. Internet services such as MedLine and its free compani
bMed allow easy keyword searching through millions of journal articles. Y
en have to pay to see the whole article, although if you contact the pub
ations representative of the publisher, he or she might send it to you free
arge. Again, public and academic libraries may provide free access to ca
lders or students.
 Unlike general-interest publications, journals are written for narrow
diences. In the case of business journals, the audience could be invest
oking for safe bets for their money; in the case of medical journals, it co
 researchers and healthcare providers seeking information on the latest tre
nt for cancer. The specialized audience means that a journal’s language te

```

```
 r, offer a forum in which issues are discussed that fall below the radar of

tream-media outlets.

es

sily overlooked source of secondary material is the university thesis.
shed theses are valuable not only for the information they contain (and
bibliographies), but also for the insights they can provide into the writer.
 is prepared by someone who has since become a prominent figure, or
rget of your investigation, can show you a side of that person’s character
ight not get anywhere else. Often, these papers were written before their
s started to manicure their image or control access to information about
lves. Theses are typically maintained by the library at the institution
 thor attended. You can also search for them online at www.collections

s

 journalists and newsworthy individuals save important information for
-length treatment. These books tend to slip under the radar and fail to
t from wide publicity, except, of course, within the readerships to which
re geared. Books of this sort resemble trade magazines, in that they are
ned to be read by a select audience. Most don't become bestsellers. They
pecially likely to disappear quickly from the public eye when published by
onal or small press.

iographies

er gem of the research library is the bibliography—not just the list of
nces printed at the end of a book or essay, but the separate lists of pub materials relating to a given topic. It’s often worth enlisting the help of
ference librarian in navigating the available resources. One of the most
 aspects of bibliographies is that, although they are intended to guide you

Electronic Sources

nline encyclopedias

nline encyclopedias can provide invaluable introductions to var
ubjects, distilling a significant amount of information into a relati
hort text. Encyclopedia articles can be good starting points for all kind
esearch projects. They vary in accuracy and reliability, however. Some,
s the Canadian Encyclopedia (www.thecanadianencyclopedia.com)
ncyclopaedia Britannica (www.britannica.com), are expanded online vers
f encyclopedias that used to exist in book form. Both have rigorous edi
al processes that produce work that is highly reliable, though you woul
enerally include material from these sources without citing them. The on
anadian Encyclopedia is free, while Encyclopaedia Britannica online requir
odest paid subscription. Of course, the best known encyclopedia in our e
robably Wikipedia, which is completely free but of inconsistent reliability.
crowdsourced, meaning that the articles can be written by anyone, and w
 large online community acts to correct mistakes, it still may contain
ias and errors. On the other hand, Wikipedia boasts an impressive num
f entries, many on obscure topics. It can be a useful starting place, so lon
esearchers confirm any details they find there.

 ebsites

ost institutions and businesses have websites that advertise their ware
ublicize their point of view. As such, the sites function as expanded n
eleases, in some cases disguised as real news. That said, some sites can be
ul repositories of valuable information, posting news articles and lists of
o other relevant websites. For instance, a law firm's website may link to art
r documents related to its current legal battles, thereby providing a ha
eference library for journalists just getting up to speed on an issue.

Social-networking sites

he explosion of Facebook, Twitter, Tumblr, Instagram, LinkedIn, and o

```
information, track unfolding issues, and locate people. After the 2013

on practice for reporters to go to Facebook after people die accidentFriends often set up memorial sites with information about the deceased

A targeted online search can help discover these gems. On Google, sim
nter the name of the person you are searching, then add “site:urlofsite
the keywords (e.g., “Bob Smith site:Facebook.com’). This will even tur
ings such as whether the person you are searching has “liked” a publi
ook post, giving you a sense of the person’s preferences and connections.
ith any information of this type, careful checking is required before
d in a broadcast or write-up, but there is no question that online socia
rks have become important resources for journalists.

ing lists

ng lists—or listservs—are discussions to which you must formally sub
. Think of the specialty publications discussed earlier. If you were to tak
 the professionals who subscribe to the publications, gather them in
 and then be a fly on the wall, you would be able to glean a great dea
 the conversations. With a listserv, instead of gathering such people i
sical space, their discussion is in cyberspace. The participants converse

ils, which are sent out to all members of the listserv. Just as there ar
lty publications for many professions, so, too, are there listservs for ever
ivable group. The listservs allow healthcare advocates, lawyers, victims o
 and—yes—journalists, to dispense advice, dispel rumours, and debate
sues.
s with many a group conversation, there tend to be people who dominate
oceedings while the others simply listen. But you don't have to be a memf the trade or occupation in question to join the mailing list. On many

iscussion groups

scussion groups and forums are similar to listservs in that they consist

me groups serve local communities and post news about upcoming eve
 t, while of little interest to the mainstream media, may help with a parti
 investigation. Unlike listservs, discussion groups and forums are more
out membership. The groups can be found using the regular search engin
d you can join them more or less immediately.
 Easy membership has its advantages and disadvantages. Reporters hunt
r information or people for their stories can usually dip in and out of
scussion groups without having to submit to the potentially time-consumi
ocess of obtaining approval from a moderator, as is the case with listservs.
u were looking for someone who was still smarting from being victimi

sting of car enthusiasts who regularly swap war stories about good deals
d deals. And if you're really interested in having a particular person in y
ory, or in using him or her as a potential contact, you can post a questi
questing that the person respond directly to your email address. It can
at easy.
 The downside is that the discussion can easily veer off topic, or it can
minated by people who have little positive or interesting to say, forcing
 t of the people in the group into a stony, self-imposed silence. An additio
d growing problem is the amount of unsolicited advertising messages
any newsgroups. The way to make the discussion groups work for you i
end a bit of time looking for the ones that meet your needs.
  Online discussion forums bring together communities of people wit
ommon interest, such as aviation. Some have restricted membership, such
 professionals in a particular field. They are usually searchable and may
re reliable than traditional discussion groups.

 s a place on the Internet that contains information the owner finds rel

as able to find some sources that were perfectly suitable, had somehing interesting to say and away we went and did the television story.
o there’s a case where the Internet was where I found the inspirtion for the story, then my blog became a helpful tool in finding

l secondary-source information should be independently veried. An investigator must check later issues of the publication for

e original sources. But secondary sources can provide an invaluable

 Governments run on paperwork, producing an endless stream of infor
n. Some of it is available online, free of charge. Much of it is not, includ
 array of information and data of particular interest to researchers and j
ists. From daycare inspection reports to environmental studies to inte
dits, governments monitor their own activities closely. But they aren't alw
en to outside scrutiny and accessing public records locked inside gove
nt filing cabinets isn’t always easy.
 Predictably, the more potentially damaging a record, the more pri
vernments tend to be about releasing it. The corollary rule is that the m
ficult a record is to obtain, the greater the likelihood it contains infor
on of genuine interest and concern. There can exist a-culture-of secrec
nadian bureaucracies; it isnt uncommon for officials to avoid criticis
emselves or their superiors by withholding information that could p
maging or embarrassing. The principle of self-protection is entirely und
andable, of course. But when it comes to the operation of public instituti
ch as governments, it becomes problematic. Democratic institutions m
 open and transparent. Governments may be the custodians. of public infor aoa bl ; eae fre)

ovide reasonable public access to the records that document the conduc
blic business. —
 Levels of government openness vary from municipality to municip
y and between federal and provincial departments. The Internet age
ened up unprecedented possibilities for governments to share storehou
 information with citizens. Records that once sat in dusty rooms can
 placed online for fast, widespread, and cheap public access. Some gove
nt departments and agencies in Canada have seized that opportunity.
deral and provincial departments, and the vast majority of municipalit
ve websites that contain stock information about their activities. So
ich we will explore in this chapter, have gone beyond promotional mate
d press releases to provide information of real use to researchers
urnalists.
 While Canadian bureaucracies were initially tentative in their use of
ternet as a transparency tool, a steady flow of information has moved on
 the benefit of the public interest and the work of journalists who take
me to look deeply. The days of doing the “doublespeak tango” with bure
ats in order to get a basic public report aren't over—not nearly—but, at

lustered around it to widen the image.

ewspaper databases and archives

ere are other sources for documenting the words and arguments of el
ficials. Reporters are usually present during political debates, recording
anscribing, and their work shows up in newspapers and broadcast ou
ery day. They may quote only a few lines from an entire day of debate.
ose quotations are generally designed to summarize the central debate

flect the most important comments.
  Online editions that allow you to read up-to-the-minute reports and ar
low you to research recent articles. But only newspaper archive databases,

s been said on the public record—in news stories, broadcast news transcr
nd press releases along with numerous news sources. Such subscription
tabases can be extremely expensive. But many university and public libr
nd news organizations have subscriptions with news archive services.

hat They Get

ne ever-present consideration for journalists who research public-inte
sues is the hidden influences on political decisions. Why, for example,
rticular elected official lobby on behalf of a particular firm for a govern
ntract? Why does a certain politician vote consistently against environmen

otection measures?
  As much as we might hope political decisions are made in the p
terest, a heart-sinking array of evidence exists to the contrary. Someti
e motivations behind political actions can be traced to financial suppor
liticians. Political contribution data is one of the most basic tools for e
ning political motivations. Elected officials who receive financial support
orporations, unions, and other organizations may be seen to be influence
e political or social aims of those donors.
  A mining company or developer that lavishes a politician with the fina
upport needed to get elected might expect its interests to be protected
ndustry-unfriendly legislation is proposed and voted on in Parliament. Pol
ontribution records are one way of studying the underlying forces behind
onduct of elected representatives. Political contribution data can somet
how links between elected officials and people in the community, which
elp shed light on the hidden reasons shaping political decision-making.

ditors’ reports

deral sponsorship scandal in which public money was poured into adve
ng agencies to promote Canadian federalism: “Rules were broken or ignor
very stage of the process for more than four years, Fraser said in a state
eaturing remarkably strong language. “And there was little evidence of
eceived for the money spent.”!®

ste. It triggered a controversial federal government inquiry that rea

obbyists

rm-twisting once occurred in private, but disclosure rules in Canada have
ut lobbying on the public record federally and in some provinces.
  There is nothing illegal or inappropriate about the act of lobb
ecision-makers; the rules just require that lobbyists provide information
hemselves and the subjects on which they are attempting to influence
ians. The Lobbyists Registration Act covers only people who are paid to l
ncluding company employees for whom lobbying government represen
ignificant part of their duties (20 per cent of their time or more).
  Lobbyists must now update their filings every six months. Volunteer l
harities are required to file annual statements with the federal governm
utlining their structure, directors, and financial information—how m
oney they’ve received, the sources of that income, and what they've
ith it. It isn’t exactly exhaustive public scrutiny, but it can provide an in
to organizations that vow to do public interest work with money from
eaning donors.

Corporate records
For federally registered associations and companies, Industry Canada

tains an online database (www.oupcanada.com/dd/link4.31) that is sear
by keyword. A company name alone will turn up a list of records includin
mal name, corporation number, address, incorporation date, status, and
of directors. Unlike many provincial registries, the federal one doesn't p
the addresses of directors, but you can file a written request to Corpora
Canada for that information.
  Information on corporations registered provincially is public and gen
accessible at provincial ministries that handle corporate affairs. Some
inces, such as Ontario, have contracts with private firms that charge the
for conducting searches of the database.
  Companies that do business across the country or across borders
to be registered federally while smaller firms that operate within a cit
province are more likely to turn up in provincial registries. In some prov
corporate search records also indicate any documents filed by the corpor
which could detail anything from changes in directors to articles of inco
tion. This is useful because a corporate record otherwise lists only the c
makeup of a corporation's directors and officers. Just because a particular
doesn't show up on a current list of officers and directors doesn’t mean
never there. You may be interested in knowing whether someone was in
with the company in the past and subsequently resigned, or you may
wish to know the names of previous officers and directors. If details or
changes have not been filed by the corporation, you will need a point-i
report. By choosing a past date, you can, in some provinces, search a his
record of a corporation's officers and directors. A point-in-time searc
directors and officers at the time along with historical information inc
amalgamations and name changes.
   It’s also important to note that the names of those on corporate filings
always the people running the company. Those who wish to remain anon
can list colleagues, partners, and others in corporate filings while continu
exercise control from behind the scenes. Getting at the real influence be
company doesn't always end with a corporate search.

  Before heading to the courthouse . . . the investigative journalist
  needs to have a basic understanding of laws and regulations and
  where to find them.

The structure of the courts

  The provinces and territories are responsible for the lower rungs
court hierarchy, which are known collectively as the inferior courts.
courts have limited powers and jurisdiction, and generally deal with less-s
crimes and regulatory offences. Provincial court (or territorial court
territories) is where everyone accused of a crime makes an initial appea
and is arraigned on charges. Persons accused of minor crimes usually
trial in this court, while those facing serious charges can choose to be tr
superior court. In murder cases, defendants are arraigned in provincial
but the trial must be held in superior court. Youth courts follow procedur
out in the Youth Criminal Justice Act to deal with young people who are ch
with crimes. Small claims courts hold informal hearings to settle civil di
involving modest amounts of money and bankruptcy courts deal with cred
claims when individuals and businesses become insolvent. In some prov
family courts address access and custody issues when couples separate.

Court websites

Most courts maintain their own websites. The information offered varie
the typical site provides access to dockets, news releases, a searchable da
of rulings, and links to laws and background information about the
system. The documents in a court file are rarely available in electronic f
although this is changing as courts move to allow the filing of electroni
sions of documents. In the meantime, journalists can visit the court r
where the file is held to view and copy documents.
To know where to look for files, journalists need to understand the basic
ture of the courts and the kinds of cases they hear.’
  The highest echelons of the courts are known as superior courts.
include the Supreme Court of Canada (the country’s highest court), the
of appeal of each province/territory, and the top level of trial court in each
ince/territory—which is known as the Superior Court, the Supreme Court,

and jury preside over trials at this level. All serious crimes, major civil cases
legal challenges are heard in the superior courts. Ottawa also maintains th
eral court system, which consists of a trial and appeal court, and deals with
that involve federal legislation. Income-tax disputes, immigration appeals
the seizure of ships are examples of cases heard in the Federal Court of Can

Qualified privilege and court documents

A legal principle known as “qualified privilege” applies to all informatio

```

```
ilege, which also exists for the debates of Parliament and legislatures,
that parties and witnesses can speak freely and frankly—and the media
rt what is said—without fear of being sued for libel. Journalists must
mind that it is the forum in which the information is made public that
es whether it is privileged, not the nature of the information itself.
ection of privilege stops at the courtroom door; if allegations made
the protected forum are reported, both the source and the media outlet
ued.
 immunity from lawsuits is described as “qualified”; in other words,
lege comes with strings attached. A news report of what is said in the
om must be a “fair and accurate” representation of what happened.
ia are not expected to produce verbatim accounts, but reports must
lly correct and reflect any contradictory information presented. “The
 one media law textbook advises, “must be substantially correct and
 same meaning as a word-for-word account.”!> There must be no sugthat the journalist published the information out of spite or for other
 motives. Conduct seen by the courts as evidence of malice includes
 key facts and publishing information the journalist knows to be false.'®
ified privilege also applies to news and investigative reports based on
ts filed with the courts. The rulings of courts, tribunals, and official
 are covered, as are the contents of documents produced or discussed
court or before a tribunal or inquiry. The Supreme Court of Canada has
 qualified privilege to fair and accurate news reports of pleadings—the
 statement of claim and the defendant’s written response—filed with
s in civil actions.!? When reporting on pleadings, journalists should
 that none of the allegations has been proven in court and must make
 to contact the target of the allegations for a response. These steps add
to the story and fulfill the legal requirement for a news report to be fai
 ate.
 protection from libel lawsuits makes court documents of particular
to investigative reporters. They are the building blocks for producing
ing stories that are solid, factual, and libel-proof.

Criminal Case Documents

When criminal charges are laid, court staffers open a file and assign a n
to the case. Journalists have the same right as any other citizen to inspe
at the court's registry during office hours. Each file is identified by numbe
indexed by the surname of the defendant or the name of the company
prosecuted. Newer files can be searched at registries on a computer da
but journalists may find that a card index is used to keep track of olde
(Note as well that older files are put into long-term storage at a separat
tion, or transferred to the provincial archives for preservation and catal
and may take longer to retrieve.) There is generally a fee to retrieve each

to photocopy documents.
  The Supreme Court of Canada, based in Ottawa, offers online ac
its docket, rulings, legal briefs, and other information about cases on i
site. Provinces are divided into judicial districts, with a superior court

```

location of court offices and registries. If the court office is located i

aff to photocopy or fax documents. This usually involves a fee.

  A sealing order can be challenged. Journalists and other interested

asking that the order be rescinded and all or part of the file be made pu
Ontarios Court of Appeal has described orders sealing a search warra
other document as a “significant intrusion” on freedom of expression. App
the Dagenais/Mentuck test, the court ruled in 2003 that warrants shou
sealed only when there is a serious risk to someone’s right to a fair trial
administration of justice. The police had claimed that publicity would
heir efforts to question witnesses, but the court said these concerns we
vague and freedom of expression should not be sacrificed “to give po

under suspicion for processing tainted meat, with minor editing to shie
identity of a confidential informant. Just as significantly, the court sa

the case for keeping all or part of a file in the public domain. The messa
ournalists is, do not take no for an answer; if the search warrant file of

he suspect's privacy interests should prevail. The suspect had been acq

he man’s trial. The Supreme Court's decision set out rules for access to
ts. A judge should take into account the type of evidence, privacy int
nd whether the exhibit has been produced in a courtroom. As well,

Rulings

The verdict, the sentence imposed on a convicted offender, and other
decisions a judge makes during a criminal case are usually produced in
and a copy is added to the court file. Rulings provide a snapshot of the ev
presented in a case and how the judge interpreted the law. They also provi
names of the lawyers for each party, who may be willing to help the jou
track down more information about the case or set up interviews wit
clients. The Canadian Law List offers an online database of contact infor
for lawyers across Canada (www.canadianlawlist.com), which can be se
by name, location, or legal speciality.
  Since precedents form the basis for our common-law system of justi
nificant decisions are published in bound volumes, known as case-law repor
future reference. Commercial publishers like QuickLaw offer searchable dat
of rulings dating back many decades for a fee, but the Supreme Court of C
and courts in many provinces offer free, online access to their recent ruling

Canadian Legal Information Institute (www.canlii.org) offers free online
to the full text of rulings of courts in all provinces. Access varies but the
tion includes decisions dating back to the early 1990s, with older rulings
scanned into the system and new ones being posted as they are release
Supreme Court of Canada and other courts have a practice of notifying the
of the release of decisions in high-profile cases, and procedures may be in p
make other rulings public soon after the parties involved have been notified

Pre-sentence reports

These reports are prepared to give the judge as complete a picture as p
about an offender and whether he or she is a good candidate for reha
tion. Probation officers interview relatives, former employers, and
officers in order to compile reports that explore, in detail, the offender's
background, work history, criminal record, and past behaviour. Pre-se
reports are usually public but some courts reserve the right to seal all
of a report if it invades another person's privacy or includes sensitive m
or psychological assessments. A journalist investigating the backgroun
repeat offender or a criminal involved in a shootout with police will find
able information in the person's latest pre-sentence report.

blication Bans and Other

ling orders

rior court judges have the power to seal documents presented as ev
e or filed with the court. While such orders are more common in ci
ns, they have been imposed in criminal cases. Three provinces—Ontario
toba, and Prince Edward Island—give judges sweeping powers to seal a
 ment filed in a civil case.#* Under the rules of the Federal Court of Canad
 ty can apply to a judge for an order to keep any document confidential.“
 In the wake of the Dagenais ruling, judges must balance the right of acce
 st privacy concerns or commercial interests when deciding whether
 documents. In reviewing a sealing order sought under the Federal Court
, the Supreme Court of Canada ruled in 2002 that confidentiality should
ted only to prevent “a serious risk to an important interest.” The federal go
ent wanted to prevent an environmental group from seeing informati
t the sale of nuclear reactors to China. The Chinese government claim
ned the information and the contract required Atomic Energy of Canad
eep it confidential. The Supreme Court ruled the information should
 d because a commercial interest, one with wide implications for the publ
est, was at stake: a confidentiality agreement under a business contract.*
 Most confidentiality concerns arise in civil cases that attract little or
a attention, and sealing orders have been sought and imposed with lit
rd to the public interest. A Saskatchewan judge sealed the entire file o
 case in order to protect the contents of a few documents. The order sto
 ine years until a second judge opened the file and ruled that court fi
ld not be sealed “other than in exceptional circumstances.’* Such reaso
 ot only bodes well for journalists’ attempts to overturn sealing orders, b
ould mean fewer documents would be sealed in the first place.

s in civil cases

es presiding over civil cases have the power to ban publication of informa

ented during a dispute between private litigants may be seen as having lit
o public interest. Laws in Ontario, Manitoba, and Prince Edward Isla

justice to any person. If a hearing is closed, judges have the power to iss

ow range of circumstances.”® Courts have banned publication of the name
ople involved in lawsuits that allege sexual abuse, because there is a real t
ausing humiliation or serious damage to reputations. But embarrassmen
e is not grounds for shielding the identity of the parties involved in lawsuit
he Supreme Court of Canada has said, “the sensibilities of the individua
lved” in a case is no basis for deviating from the principle of openness.
Publication restrictions have also been put in place to protect a child, t
y of a witness, details of patents, or a company’s trade secrets, and ha
 used when “the administration of justice would be rendered impracticab
he presence of the public.”” The whole point of an action claiming damage
heft of confidential business information, for instance, would be defeated
plaintiff had to reveal the information in court.

any being investigated is based. The next step is determining whether

he American court system is complex and jurisdiction over certain offen

ral courts

tting Justice Records to Work

nding and backgrounding people

t people can be tracked down through phone listings or a city director

he case who may be willing to point a reporter in the right direction.
 Court records are an unsurpassed resource for compiling backgroun
ormation about a person. Has the person sued or been sued? Has he or s

on's name in the Canadian Legal Information Institute's online database

rt rulings in all provinces and territories (www.canlii.org). The results w

ll percentage of court cases culminate in written rulings—but may turn
se or two to kick-start an investigation.

ialized Tools

cal files

public libraries maintain vertical files—a collection of indexed folders
ning material on a wide range of subjects. They are a key source of infor about local history, prominent people who have lived or made their
in the area, and local, regional, or national issues of importance to the
ity. The public library in Penticton, British Columbia, for instance,

 ns a large vertical file collection that includes subjects such as acid rain,

l care, forests and forestry, dude ranches, and the Yukon. Meanwhile,
in branch of the Halifax Regional Library has files on the city’s history,
usinesses, and civic issues like downtown development and the sewage
 nt project for Halifax Harbour. A typical vertical file contains a hodge of information—newspaper clippings, magazine articles, pamphlets,
ports—all of it compiled over the years and much of it difficult, if not
ble, to find anywhere else. Files may contain obscure ephemera like old
ant menus, railway tickets, and transit schedules. Vertical files are not
hensive but they are readily accessible, easy to use, and a good startnt for an investigation with a local or historical angle. Vertical files are
 accessible through the library's reference desk.

nterlibrary loans

n the wake of the 1992 explosion that killed 26 men at Nova Scotia’s West
oal mine, sources advised reporters for The Chronicle Herald to check
he safety record of the mine's top manager. The manager, they suggested,
een criticized during an inquiry into the deaths of four men at an Albe
ine a decade earlier. But hard evidence proved elusive. The manager was
entioned in newspaper accounts of the earlier disaster or in the inquiry’s

```

```
gs, which was tucked away in the library of the Alberta Department o
 in Edmonton. Ordering a photocopy of the huge document would have
expensive but the department's librarian suggested an interlibrary loan
 the newspaper's in-house library as the destination. Within days, th
ers were thumbing through the transcripts, which were peppered with
sm of the manager and his hard-nosed management style. The borrowed
 ipts became the basis of a front-page exposé that was nominated for th
al Newspaper Award for enterprising reporting.’
 terlibrary loan may be the best option for tracking down books and govnt documents that have been identified using Google Books, WorldCat,
ther online tools, but are not available through local libraries or on th
t. Anyone with a library card can borrow materials from any library i
, the United States, or other countries. There will likely be a small fee fo
equest and an additional fee to obtain photocopies of shorter items, such
 cles from magazines and journals. Books, government reports, news on microfilm, dissertations, and some microfilmed archival materials
e borrowed, but reference works and rare books are usually excluded.
y staff will locate the material and make the application on your behalf.
es that have a copy of the book, article, or report you are seeking can
ntified through LAC’s online catalogue (collectionscanada.gc.ca/amicus).
 the site by title, author, or subject, then click the locations tab.
he drawback to using an interlibrary loan is time. It could be days or even
 or two before the material arrives, but you may be able to arrange fo
delivery by paying an additional fee. Libraries supplying articles, chapters
ks, and other shorter items may prefer to send photocopies or scans o
ges being sought, speeding up the process.

ng Archives

val resources

 of archives as the attics of the nation. They preserve government docu yellowed newspapers, older court files and property records, photos,
 film and sound recordings, genealogical records, and the diaries and
al papers of famous and ordinary Canadians alike. They offer tangible
o the nation’s past.
e LAC collection in Ottawa is vast—it has millions of pages, plus tens o
nds of hours of film and sound recordings. The collection of architectural
and drawings alone comprises 2.5 million items dating to the sixteenth
. It holds material of national importance, including the records of th

```

```
 Canadian history. In addition, each province has its own archives, hou
g local records and the personal papers and diaries of many individuals a
milies. Libraries, museums, and some institutions and corporations mainta
chives, too. Many of these holdings, as noted below, can be searched on
chive’s website and, in some cases, the records and images themselves h
en digitized and posted online.
 Most records, however, still must be searched on-site, so a research trip w
kely be required. Most archives offer lists of researchers who will do the wo
 your behalf, for a fee. If the search is specific and can be conducted witho
o much effort, archives staff will respond to written or emailed requests
formation about specific records or subjects that may be preserved in th
llections. Staff at LAC field thousands of written requests each month a
m to reply within 30 days. There is no fee for this service, and requests can
bmitted by letter, fax, or by using an electronic form available at the Libr
