```
       Researching and
   1 Finding People

  Recognize that the more organized and methodical you are in
  building your file of material, the more useful it will prove to be
  when you begin to write.

ntroduction

he document that would launch a sweeping investigation into allegation
ection fraud and mysterious automated phone calls to voters during the 2
deral election campaign couldn't have been more unremarkable: a rune-mill phone bill. But getting to it for the Ottawa Citizen’s Glen McGre
nd Postmedia’s Stephen Maher couldn't have been more circuitous. Once
eld it in their hands, it would lead to places neither of them—or the mill
f Canadians who followed their tale—could have imagined.
  The two journalists had been following the strange story of so-called r
alls—automated calls made to thousands of Canadians in ridings across
ountry with misleading messages about poll locations. The Ottawa-ba
eporters interviewed voters who had been quoted in local media. They foll
ads. They built a database detailing the ridings and whatever information
n the record about candidates, volunteers, and questionable calls. Gradua
he information began pointing to Guelph, Ontario, as ground zero for s
ighly suspicious activity apparently designed to keep non-Conservative
orters from finding their way to the ballot box.
  And then, a tip: Sources pointed them to an Edmonton company as
ource of the automated calls to Guelph voters.
  Those phone calls, the reporters knew, were documented somewhere.
ick was figuring out how to get that evidence. The answer ultimately c
y following the money. Campaign office phone bills, routinely submitte
lections Canada, detail all campaign spending. And phone service is a c
aign expense. A visit to the Elections Canada office allowed the reporter
eview the itemized bills along with other election campaign spending rece
he most revelatory of the bills showed two calls from Guelph to the Edmon
```

```
was the unlikely breakthrough they needed. The investigation was on.

hatever story you're working on, you need to find out what kind of
perwork has to get filed and with whom,” says McGregor. “Whether

cords somewhere. Quite often these documents are in the public
main. The key is find out what and where they are and figure out
w to get them.”!

 reporters dug in, more public documents eventually emerged to open

n which police laid out their case for court orders empowering them t
hone records from Internet and phone companies.
the end, the reporters discovered that police investigators had traced th
ent robocalls that played havoc with Guelph voters to a Virgin Mobile
ble cellphone registered to a fictional character named Pierre Poutine,

e political fallout was seismic, triggering charges against a campaign

iticians that used the calls without proper attribution and strict new
s designed to clamp down on such fraudulence in the future.
ere's a great Woody Allen quote about 85 per cent of success is jus

to see the document.”
ve you ever wondered how a journalist finds such detailed information on

so show how you can use the Internet, databases, and other resources t
esearch subjects, sources, and experts relevant to your project.
 we live and breathe, we create an information trail. Everything from
rsonal loans to your driving record is on file somewhere. Most of that
```

```
  People, after all, are always at the centre of news. And that means journal

  There are some basic questions to ask yourself before you begin the

hat is their relationship to others who might be relevant to the broader
oure researching? Where does this person fit in the larger context of the s

nformation? What does your subject do for a living? Is he or she a membe

xotic dancer? Have they had brushes with the law that would have produ
ourt records? What has your subject written that might be publicly availa
utting together this sort of detailed character sketch is a good place to
Human activities and relationships are often the starting point to hie
hat records might exist and where to look for them.
  While our focus in this chapter is on public records and the Internet, i

ny detailed look into the characters at the centre of an important story sh
```

```
  Begin with this rule: General search bars are for amateur researchers.
xist for teens looking for fast access to the latest music video. Not you.
  Experienced Web searchers need to be more precise, using advanced se
ptions that filter out much of the irrelevant material. All of the major se
ngines provide instructions for navigating their particular advanced searc
’s worth learning the skills for several of them. No single search engine co
he whole Internet; most search only a small portion. The more engines
se, the broader your coverage.
  Google obviously reigns as the most popular search tool on the Web.
he standard Google search bar entirely and go straight to the advanced se
creen—it allows for far more control and precision in your search. We'll
uss below a number of search operators that allow you to filter out the sl
nd pinpoint the material you really want. And remember that while Goo
 great, you need to expand your search beyond a single source. Try plug
he same search terms into a range of reliable search engines, and you'll l
ee entirely new results and an ever-widening scope of sources. (A sampl
f alternative search tools can be found here: www.oupcanada.com/d
ink7.1) ©)
  “The most basic advice I can give for effective Web searching is t
atient,” says Astrid Lange, head librarian at the Toronto Star who spends
ays researching facts, documents, and data for reporters. “Don't expe
earch engine to give you the answers you need in its first few results.”
  While Google's algorithm ranks results by “importance,” it’s quite poss
hat your dream document doesn't fit into that formula, she says. “Go bey
he first few pages of results, and click on anything that looks useful. The an
ou need may be buried on the 10th page of results.”
  Also be aware that search engines like Google and Bing customize
esults based on your search history (known as filter bubbles). To avoid
lear your cookies and cache regularly, or try a search engine like DuckDuck
hich avoids filter bubbles, Lange advises.
  While the specific instructions for conducting advanced searches d
rom engine to engine, the general principles are largely consistent. Goo
or example, offers many of these targeted searches on its Advanced Se
age (find the link to the right of the main search bar in Google). With o
earch engines, you can type the operations into the main search fields
ou understand the proper format. Check the help pages to find the part
ar operators each engine supports. The features essentially allow you to
eyond simple keyword searches in an effort to isolate information.
```

```
uthor
One of the less used—but highly valuable—searches for your checklist is fi

ocation is a geographically based search operator that isolates, for exam

ews. Add the term location:Canada to keywords, such as genetic rese
and Google News will filter the results for Canadian content. You can als
his with provincial abbreviations, such as NS for Nova Scotia or BC for Br

Subject and topic directories
The best stories always pull the camera lens back on an issue’s big picture.
orest gives the tree context. That’s where subject directories come in. T
ools give you the kind of fast overview that will give you ideas, resources,
confidence that you're telling a tale that fits within a larger narrative. The
uthoritative, often curated, built on a public good instead of a profit mo
and are sufficiently broad in scope to serve as a strong starting point for

ngine. A sampling of the subject and topic directories available online ca
found here: www.oupcanada.com/dd/link7.2 ©)

A final note on Web searches
Websites can prove a useful research tool for finding out about people, and
ust because of the site’s content. Tools exist to allow researchers to dig
information that isn’t immediately apparent. At the sites www.domaintools.
r www.whois.net, for instance, an online directory lets you access informa
about the people behind any given website. Researchers can obtain informa
about the person or company name associated with a site, along with co
information. Of course, sites are often registered in the names of tech
contacts or agents rather than the individuals who actually run the organiza
posting the site, but this contact information may prove useful in certain

```

```
he site you're looking for has disappeared, it may not be gone forever.
ne archiving tool at www.archive.org can help you access websites that
en pulled down. By typing in the address, you'll get a list of dates on
he database archived the site. Click on a dated version of the site, and
l show up. It is, however, a hit-and-miss process.
ause it’s easy to remove a website from the Internet instantly, it’s always
idea to save a copy of a site onto your hard drive if you know it’s going
mportant to your research. In some cases, people will pull their site
nce they get a sense that authorities or journalists are looking into their
. If you haven't saved a copy, it may be difficult to prove what had been
previously. The best method here is doing a screen grab of the site b
n image of what is on screen to your hard drive.

 alerts

discussed earlier in this book, online news alerts are an increasingly
tool for journalists, especially those who work on long-term projects
d to keep track of what’s happening in their area of interest. Services
 Google Alerts (www.google.com/alerts) and Yahoo! Alerts (alerts.
om) will serve up daily news headlines and stories based on your
s.
ting up Internet news alerts is one of the first things Julian Sher does
e begins an investigation. At any given time, he’s got at least three or four
livering stories to his email every day. “If you're working on a long pro want to be able to follow it as it evolves,” he says. “It gives you leads you
ould have thought of because names of people and experts come up.””

 directories

 experts was a lot more work before the Internet came along. Now,
ers and journalists have an amazing array of options for getting fas
o people with impressive expertise on virtually any subject, from the
mpact of Madonna to the technicalities of bridge engineering.
tever you're researching, someone out there is spending a lot of time
```

```
 Searching media databases can be daunting. Putting a couple of keyword
 a database can turn up thousands of hits. So you'll need to develop tech
ues for limiting such searches to find the major pieces you need to know
sider limiting the results to stories that are 1000 words or over; this filt

 Also, consider the less-obvious information published in the media, suc
obituaries. Julian Sher recalls the challenge of finding the family of a dea
 t from Halifax with a common surname.
 “We did the standard directory searches in Halifax, then moved outwar
Nova Scotia and then the Maritimes,” he recalls. “My editor suggested qui
ely that we do a special search for the obit. Sure enough, in the obit, th
tioned all the next of kin and then we found the mother. And from t
her we were able to find the brother and we got a great interview. >
 Be sure to save or copy important stories from media databases, as you wi
 ly need to consult them later. Mark them up. Highlight key facts. Put ques
 marks around issues you need to explore. Circle names of people you'
t to talk to. Spending a few hours—or days—just reading is likely the be
 to get your brain thinking critically about how to proceed.
```

```
sumés

sumés are the calling cards of the working world. They also document ho
ple choose to represent themselves to potential employers. It’s one of t
to documents Rosner is always looking for.
 “This is an undervalued resource, says Rosner. “It provides a road ma
a person’s life and career, and is invaluable in any investigation. It is oft
d with public applications, and therefore is often publicly available.’?”? Whe
her-and-son entrepreneurs Michael and Mark Gobuty moved their federal
ded garment factory from Manitoba to Florida in the early 1990s, Rosne

the city who had been an owner of the Winnipeg Jets NHL hockey club and
ocal racetrack. He and son Mark started the garment factory after securing
n from the federal Business Development Bank.’®
 “In general, it’s possible to build a road map of any person's life by tho
ghly analyzing public records,” says Rosner. Using those records, th
owed that the Gobutys reneged on a federal bank loan and misrepresente
mselves to Florida officials, who provided them with a host of financi
entives to move south.”
 Michael Gobuty’s resumé, which the reporters obtained from a filing wi
ederal immigrant investor program, contained a number of valuable pieces
ormation that assisted the investigation. For example, it included exact dat
his involvements with his various companies, which allowed reporters
rt a chronology of his career. It contained his personal references, includi
nitoba premier Gary Filmon and ambassador Ken Taylor (then Canadia
bassador to the United States). A check with those people allowed the jou
ists to say in their story that Filmon had never given Gobuty permission
 his name.
 “Tt also listed as a professional reference Iain Brown, president and CEO
st National Bank in the Turks and Caicos Islands, an interesting contact
uty, who claimed around this same time to have annual income of less th
00 per year,’ says Rosner.
 Resumé controversies happen more than you might imagine, especial
en journalists take the time to background the claims.
```

```
able from sources such as the person's colleagues.
As with other documents, don't assume the information contained
mé is true. You should report only information you have verified. Resum
pen up research possibilities, however, and often mention references w
 e added to your interview list.
```

```
ens, property records, and assessment

lowing the money is always a core mission in the backgrounding of indivi
s and companies. And that pursuit is often intricately connected to lien a
perty records. The supplementary information provided by lien records c
as useful as the main details of a person’s loan. For instance, you might fin
ject’s date of birth, address, vehicle make and model, and vehicle identific
n number, allowing you to do a vehicle record search.
 On the surface, this is the kind of basic backgrounding information t
 lead reporters to subjects who are otherwise elusive.
 Property records can be equally essential for providing addresses and oth
sonal or corporate details that aren't available anywhere else.
 But these records can also become far more than tip sheets on how to f
ple. They can point journalists to the heart of much larger stories.
 A lien record, for example, was the key to a 2013 scandal that centred
ayment of nearly $382,000 by the Canada Revenue Agency to Montrea
ed organized crime boss Nicolo Rizzuto. Court records showed the fede
ncy had issued a $1.5 million lien against Rizzuto at the time the payme
 made, making him ineligible for the reimbursement payment and rais
stions about Rizzuto’s influence with tax officials.*”
 Julian Sher, in researching The Road to Hell: How the Biker Gangs are Co
ring Canada, co-authored by William Marsden, used land-title searches acr
 country in order to show the extent of wealth among individual gang member
 “To be able to say the president of the Vancouver chapter has a hou
th over $500,000, [and] his number two had a house worth over $1 m
, was one of the cases where it was important for background but actua
came a very important part of the book,” says Sher. “It caused a big stir wh
 were able to say, ‘Look this clothing store, this restaurant, this café is own
a member of the Hells Angels. ”*
 Journalists and police relied on property records while investigating
3 scandal into some federal senators claiming hundreds of thousands
lars a year in living expenses based on having primary residences outsi
Ottawa.
 In the case of former Liberal senator Mac Harb, property records revealed
 sold a home he owned 120 kilometres outside Ottawa two years earlier.4®
 When The Globe and Mail’s Greg McArthur saw Toronto mayor Rob Fo
tray himself in US television interviews as a regular guy who shuns the r
es, he sensed a debunking opportunity.

```

```
“All of the anecdotal evidence—his luxury car, the family business
ited, the tony neighbourhood where he grew up and still lives—points t
different reality than the one he portrayed in those interviews,” McArthu
“But how to put an actual price tag on his worth?”®!
Ford's family business, Deco Labels and Tags, is a private corporatio
 locks up its financial records beyond public reach. And Canadian pr
 protection laws make personal income tax statements and stock marke
ions equally inaccessible. So McArthur and his colleagues dug into pro
records using GeoWarehouse, a Web-based database of the Ontario la
ry, and LexisNexis to locate and research any Ford family property hol
in Florida.
“The only problem with these records is that they don't offer an up-to-da

 the time they changed hands,” says McArthur.
After some brainstorming, the team decided to pull another set
 detailed assessment records from the Municipal Property Assessmen
ration, which makes all of its valuations public. With a comprehensiv
tory of properties in Canada and the US, the reporting team calculat
pressive total for Ford’s holdings: $10 million.** One key trick here, sa
thur, was knowing to search, not just for the names of the Ford fami
ers, but for the corporate entities.
“If we hadn't searched for Deco or Doug Ford Holdings Inc., we nev
 have found the commercial parcels of land, which are the family’s mos
ble properties,’ he says.

I use land title searches and lien searches all the time, but it’s usually
 or a much simpler purpose—locating people, and their home address,
o I can seek them out and interview them. But this research was a
reat reminder that the documents are so much more important.
These sorts of records, and the fine print in those records, often tell
 story—and in this case, it was a story that clearly contradicted the
arrative being promoted by Toronto's chief magistrate.°’
```

```
Privacy Concerns and Dealing
ith Confidential Information

 nvasion of privacy

he privacy rights of Canadians are not well established in law.*! As we ma
lear in this chapter, personal information about individuals is accessi
hrough an array of public records. Journalists, however, could face prosecuti
  a lawsuit if they use illegal or covert methods to collect information, har
he subjects of their investigations, or invade someone's personal privacy.
ourts are only beginning to define the scope of this form of civil action und
anadian law, and only a few cases deal directly with the newsgathering act
 ies of journalists. While the rulings and examples that follow offer insig
nto how to handle many situations where confidential information is invol
 privacy issues are at stake, journalists should seek legal advice if they
ncertain of their rights.
  In Saskatchewan, Manitoba, British Columbia, and Newfoundland
abrador, privacy legislation prohibits eavesdropping on private conversatio
talking or following someone, unauthorized use of diaries or other perso
apers, and using a person’s name or likeness to advertise products with
onsent.® These restrictions do not apply where there is a public interest
he information or the material is considered privileged under defamation
hich exempts most news coverage.
  Saskatchewan's law goes a step further and specifically exempts newsgathe
ng by journalists. The federal government introduced a law in 2000

```

```
izes the media's right to gather news; the law does not apply to anyon
collects, uses or discloses” personal information for “journalistic, artisti
rary purposes.
everal rulings have helped to define the scope of British Columbia’
y law and suggest where other courts may draw the line. A televisio
, for instance, did not invade the privacy of a businessman by airin
e of his scuffle with a camera crew during a labour dispute. While t
g lot where the scuffle occurred was private property, a judge ruled any
alking by could have witnessed it and the labour dispute was a matter
 interest.” ‘
nother British Columbia man failed to prevent the broadcast of an inter
n which he was asked about his conviction for a drug offence. The man’
al record and the fact he had been pardoned were matters of publi
 a judge ruled, and did not invade his privacy. A television station wa
n 1996 for broadcasting video of a bald man undergoing a procedure
 his hair. While the company that offered the service was found liable f
on of privacy, a court dismissed a claim against the TV station because th
ny had assured it the footage could be used.”
uebec's civil law provides a general right to sue for injuries, includin
 caused by invasion of privacy.”° In addition, the province’s Charter
 Rights and Freedoms protects privacy, confidential information, and th
y, honour, and reputation” of Quebec residents.’! A teacher won $37,50
ages in 1991 after the Montreal Gazette identified him as having AIDS
ewspaper did not name the teacher, but a court ruled enough details wer
ed to identify him and his right to keep his illness and sexual orientatio
 had been violated.” Lawyers for Karla Homolka, who served 12 year
son for manslaughter in the deaths of two teenaged girls, tried to preven
dia from reporting on her private life after her release from jail in Ju
A Quebec judge rejected the request, ruling there was a public interest
a’s crimes and she could not escape media scrutiny after her release.”
he media’s use of photos or video images of someone without his or he
edge or permission also raises privacy concerns. Photographers and vid
hers usually seek a subject's consent before an image or clip is publishe
adcast. This may not always be possible, especially if the target of a
gative article or a person accused of wrongdoing refuses to co-operate.
ter reviewing a Quebec woman's complaint about the use of her phot
strate a news article, the Supreme Court of Canada ruled that image
lic figures captured in a public place can be published or broadcas
t consent. Politicians, professional athletes, artists, and others whos
 depend on publicity must expect some intrusion into their persona

```

```
the person’s stature, who is caught up in a matter of public interest, such a
siness-fraud trial or a political scandal.”
 In provinces that do not have privacy legislation, lawsuits seeking damag
 invasion of privacy have set some limits for reporting on private lives.
ading decision describes invasion of privacy as an emerging basis for a ci
tion, and one likely to succeed only when a claimant has been subjected
arassing behaviour or an intentional invasion of privacy.’” It appears,
ample, that a journalist is entitled to remove someone's garbage from
rbside in search of information. The Supreme Court of Canada ruled in 20
at it is not an invasion of privacy for police to collect and search the garba
 a suspect. Garbage put out for collection is accessible to anyone who pass
, the court ruled, and any privacy interest in the contents is lost.”°
 Two recent cases dealing with how journalists gather information are a
structive. In 2008, an Ontario judge allowed a lawsuit claiming invasion
ivacy to proceed against The Globe and Mail and journalist Jan Wong, w
rked as a maid to research a series exploring the world of Toronto's worki
or. Wong used her real name to get a job with a cleaning service but»h
ployer, co-workers, and the service’s clients were not aware she was a jo
ist. A Toronto family sued, claiming damages for embarrassment, ment
stress, and loss of dignity, based on “deceit” and “invasion of property,’ ov
 article in which Wong described ironing oversized clothing and cleaning
iled bathroom in their “monster home.” The homeowners were not nam
t their suburb was, and the lawsuit claimed acquaintances recognized
mily based on Wong’s description of their home and belongings.
 A judge refused to dismiss the lawsuit before trial, saying a full hearing w
eded to examine the competing interests involved: the right to privacy vers
e guarantee of press freedom enshrined in the Charter of Rights and Freedo
d “the ability of investigative journalists to play their role in a free a
mocratic society.” The Globe, however, issued an apology in 2010, sayin
d Wong did not intend to identify the home's owners and expressing reg
r any harm caused by the publication of Wong's stories. The case sugges
urnalists should explore other ways to tell a story before working undercove
l as few lies as possible, and avoid detailed descriptions of people and pla
at are not central to the story.
 In another case, a Nova Scotia judge refused in 2009 to prevent
ronicle Herald from publishing a conversation between a federal minist
d one of her aides. The aide inadvertently recorded more than five hours
ndid comments about political issues and other federal MPs, then mislaid
cording device in a Parliament Hill washroom. The device was given to
rald's Ottawa correspondent, who informed the aide and repeatedly as

```

```
me aide lost her job for leaving confidential briefing notes at a television
, the journalist listened to the recording device’s contents and advised
at he would be using selections in an article. The aide sought an injunco block publication, claiming invasion of privacy and improper use of he
al property (the recording device and its contents). But the judge rule
nversation was not private—it took place during a long drive and was
ard by a chauffeur—and the journalist’s restraint showed no evidence o
ional invasion of privacy.
 t is wrong,” the judge concluded, “to deprive the press, and the public
 of remarks made privately, but not confidentially in the sense of trade
 or privileged communications, after those remarks became available
e of poor record keeping or management.” The ruling suggests courts
eriously consider the media’s right to gather news when deciding whether
y has been invaded.”

idential and stolen information

rnalist can be charged with theft of documents or with possessing
ents that have been stolen by someone else.*° As well, a journalist who
raged a source to steal documents or other property could be prosecuted
 criminal law as a party to the offence.*! But when a television reporter
harged in 1989 with possession of stolen goods—a pamphlet on the yet
released federal budget—a judge ruled that information itself cannot b
” within the meaning of criminal law. The reporter could be prosecuted
or possessing the paper the information was written on, which had littl
value, prompting the judge to toss out the charge as politically motivated
 abuse of the court process.*”
 another case, a judge ruled that a journalist who photocopied a stolen
ent was not in possession of stolen goods because information trans to a copy is not stolen.® It is a criminal offence, however, to hack int

uter system to access data or programs. It is also an offence to use
rd to hack into a computer system or to supply a password to another
 to enable that person to break into a system." The criminal charges
t against the British journalists who hacked the voicemail messages o
 figures, celebrities, and ordinary citizens in pursuit of stories show th
 consequences of such illegal and unethical practices.
ourts may have the power to prevent the media from publishing o
asting confidential information and may be able to order its return. The

ian Press, for instance, was barred from publishing stories based on
 draft of the Canadian Transportation Accident Investigation and Safet

```

```
nfidential under federal law. The news service was also ordered to hand o
e draft and any copies its journalists had made.*
  In another case, the CBC was ordered not to broadcast the contents
cuments about a controversial development project because the disclosu
uld violate solicitor-client confidentiality. The client had the legal right
rce the media to keep the information confidential.** As well, a journalist w
suses confidential information about individuals or companies risks bei
ed for any harm caused by the release of the information.*

arassment and trespassing

ile investigative journalists should be vigilant and thorough in th
search, they do not have a right to hound people for information or commen
porters are expected to seek comment from the subjects of their stories,eir efforts must be reasonable and they cannot subject people to harassment
 Making repeated telephone calls intended “to harass any person” is a cri
al offence.** A few calls does not amount to harassment; dozens made i
ort time period or after someone refuses to comment could cross the l
nd journalists who put their subjects under surveillance at their home
siness must avoid being mistaken for stalkers. The offence of stalking
iminal harassment is defined as “repeatedly following or communicating w
meone, or staking out or watching a person’s home or workplace.” Whi
ere must be evidence that the person who was targeted feared for his or
fety, there is no exemption for newsgathering and journalists cannot be c
in how their actions will be interpreted.*

  Journalists must respect private property and newsgathering does not g
em the right to trespass. They must respect fences and NO TRESPASSING si
d, if a property owner, employee, or security guard demands that they le
property, they must do so. Trespassing is considered a minor offence bu
urnalist could be fined or jailed and could also be sued for damages. A priv
ome and its outdoor property, including gardens, orchards, and farm fi
der cultivation, are considered off limits unless a person has permissi
 enter.* In Ontario, the restriction extends to a body of water on priv
operty and to ships, vehicles, trains, and aircraft. It is a criminal offence
espass on the grounds of a private home at night.”
  Journalists and camera operators can enter a property to knock on
ont door of a home or to ring the doorbell. They are also entitled to en
ores, shopping centres, transportation terminals, and the reception areas
fices. But if someone who answers the door or is in control of the prope
ders them to leave, they must comply or they can be ejected by force
```

```
Connecting the Dots

 thorough background research job attempts to trace the labyrinthine p
f a person's life, following a document trail to find how various activities
ecorded. No matter how structured and methodical your approach, your pr
ess will be determined by the information you gather, the order in whic
omes, and where it points. One piece of information inevitably leads to
ext, and you will find that the lines blur at times between researching an i
idual and investigating a company. In trying to find out about a person or
p names of new potential interviewees, therefore, your checklist of tasks
venues of investigation will draw also from the other chapters in this bo
e open to new lines of inquiry, and each time you get a relevant docume
onsider not only what it tells you but also where it might lead you.
  A name, for example, might prompt a lien search that reveals a ho
ddress and business address. A reverse search on the business address mi
rovide the name of a company. The name of the company might promp
orporate search that turns up other officers and directors who are busin
artners of the person youre researching. A lien search on the company mi
rovide still more details such as the company’s level of debt and list of cre
rs—more potential sources of information. It might also provide informat
n property or vehicles that can lead to other material from motor veh
earches and land-title inquiries. A bankruptcy search can provide details, s
s a person's net worth at a point in time and whether your subject lives in
ap of luxury or on the edge of a financial cliff.
  A basic Internet search might give you a personal website that descri
 ow this character likes to skydive. That could direct you to newsgroups
nline chat sites about skydiving (to which your subject might be a contri
or), and perhaps the discovery of other people within that online commun
ho know your subject well.
  A deep Internet backgrounding task using the tools we describe ab
an produce a thick file of messages, news stories, postings, photographs,
 ocuments about your subject dating back decades.

```

```
hatever your searches turn up, it’s important to go back and read docu
 again, days or weeks after you obtain them. Names, addresses, or company
rs that meant little to you when you first encountered them may suddenl
 lls when you examine them later with the benefit of further research.
 ere are as many possibilities for research into people as there are human
 es. The most important thing is to develop a mindset that prompts yo
 ider all of the potential research possibilities, to find alternative ways
ing information when roadblocks appear, and always to keep digging.
```

```
       Getting People to Talk

         The Art of the Interview

  The question list is the backbone of your interview. It forms the
  anticipated structure, and gives your interview strength so it can
  support whatever unexpected movements the discussion may
  make.

When Vancouver Sun reporter Lindsay Kines began investigating
unexplained disappearances of drug addicts and prostitutes from Canad
oorest neighbourhood— Vancouver's Downtown Eastside—he had to
eavily on people to find the story. On this assignment, there wasn't an ext
ive repository of documentary information ready to be unearthed by
nterprising reporter. Instead, Kines had to build trust with subjects who w
low to trust outsiders: the people of Vancouver's skid row and the police.
ended up doing hundreds of interviews.
  “Td done a lot of stories on the Downtown Eastside even before go
n the police beat, so I already had some sources there. But, really, there
o real secret to it—just spending time with people, listening to their stor
and quoting them accurately helped build trust.”! Kines would eventually
unearth the largest serial murder case in Canadian history.
  No matter how good you get at digging through documents, trolling d
ases, and unearthing archival material, a thorough investigation must invo
 alking to people. You begin dealing with people from the very beginning
 roject: experts, tipsters, government bureaucrats, clerks, secretaries, accid

 itnesses, and so on. In some cases, when not many documents are availa
human sources may give you much of your story.
  Investigative interviewing shares characteristics with that done by da
reporting journalists, but investigators are more likely to face special situati
such as interviews with reluctant or hostile subjects. Investigative report
usually do many more interviews for one story, with each interview providi
few patches of information that will be assembled into the complete quilt, s
speak. In the following sections, we'll break down the process.

```

```
 Basic Interview

 e no doubt heard that an interview is just a conversation, and the more
ual conversation it is, the more the subject will reveal. In truth, an internt much like ordinary conversation at all. Certainly, you want the tone
 milar, you may even want the person being interviewed to believe it is
ation, but an interview is actually a unique verbal form. Mastering th
 takes hard work, a dose of psychology, and a willingness to learn from
stakes.
dinary conversation darts from one topic to another, following paths
ve little to do with what was being talked about just moments before.
 ticipants are often likely to avoid a subject that creates discomfort. A
nterview, by contrast, is structured and deliberate. Its purpose—at leas
 e perspective of the journalist—is to facilitate a largely one-way transfer
rmation from source to interviewer. Difficult topics are often the whole
f the exercise.
 interview is characterized by a delicate balance of power. The interviewer
controls the topics; the interviewee controls the information. Conducting
rview effectively is a kind of verbal seduction. A big part of your job is t
 the comfort level of your subject so he or she will want to talk.
number of factors are necessary for a successful interview. You must do
 t research to prepare. Questions must be based on that research, and
ed so the interview has a logical flow. Finally, the interviewer's approach
rovoke answers that are useful for broadcast or publication. It’s a tal
 nd only a distant relative of the casual conversation. It’s hardly a surprise
 erviewing is often the task that most confounds young and experienced
sts alike.

 to Get People to Talk to You

 e, before you can put your brilliant interviewing strategy to work, you
 persuade the subject to speak to you, and not every potential interhas the same desire to share information. The head of a public-interest
may be delighted to speak about a controversial topic, but the head o
e corporation implicated with wrongdoing on the same issue may be
 . People’s willingness depends, unsurprisingly, on whether they expect
 ry to have a positive or negative impact for them. Still, if your investiga o be successful, you need to get reluctant sources to talk.
 way to do this is to be armed with information. The corporate CEO
 likely to speak to you if you already have the guts of the story. It won't

```

```
be possible for the company to halt your investigation merely by not speak
with you. In fact, you can advance the legitimate argument that it will be in
subject’s best interest to speak to you to ensure that the firm’s point of vie
heard. You also will have the compelling argument that by speaking to you,
company will help ensure the accuracy of your story.
  People like to feel important, and you can use this to your advanta
If someone is reluctant to speak to you, emphasize the significance of hi
her role in whatever issue you are investigating. One approach might be,
important that we hear from you because you probably know more about
than anyone.” Take advantage of the research you did on the person before
call to talk.
  People also like to be helpful, and journalists often need help. “Td like
help to understand this better” is one less confrontational way to appro
someone.
  If you are having particular difficulty reaching someone, or the per
is repeatedly putting you off, you may need to resort to stronger tactics.
could make it clear to your prospective interviewee that the consequence.
not speaking is to be left out of the mix and to miss the opportunity to resp
to the story’s findings, or to comments from others. This sentiment can
expressed in a positive way, by telling the source you value having his or
point of view in the story.
  Especially when a story contains findings that will cast an organizatio
person(s) in a poor light, it is important that you make every effort to obtai
interview or statement. If you get nowhere with telephone calls, and emails
direct messages are ignored, you may need to send a letter—best by regist
mail because this assures receipt is documented—repeating the request for
interview and outlining the areas you wish to cover in the interview, as
as key allegations or facts to which you are seeking a response. While this
not shield you from possible defamation action later (the best way to do th
to get the facts right, but we deal with defamation in Chapter 12), it will
establish that you made every possible effort to give the person or organ
tion a say.
  But above all, be courteous. No matter how wretched an activity is un
discussion, being accusatory will not take you far. Remember that your go
to get the information. You are not on show. Be fair in all interviews and
cussions, right from the beginning. Don’t take sides, even if your story will
a distinct point of view. Don't badmouth subjects you may talk to later. (Be
still, don't badmouth anyone.) Word of your activities will spread, and
don't want to reinforce the subject's suspicions about investigative report
Ken Metzler put it well in Creative Interviewing: The Writer's Guide to Gathe

```

```
our presence as a journalist gives them something to talk about. Afte
 interview, your reputation will precede you. That will make subsequent
ews easier or harder depending on the perception of the first ... ba
avel on express lanes.”
imately, the more you know, and the more accurately you know it, th
ikely reluctant interviewees will be to speak to you—if only to ensure
 ir own point of view is put on the record. Your career reputation
portant in this regard. If you can, point to other work you have done t
rate that you are a fair and reasonable reporter. If people understand
u do substantial, serious work, they may be more likely to speak to you.

Successful Investigative Interview

ournalists go into interviews figuring they'll just go with the flow. That’
e, especially for an investigative reporter. The investigator goes into th

 to ask

tioned earlier, a lot of interviewing is psychology. Therefore, you need
der not only what you want to know, but how what you ask, and how

e interview over.
 need to consider, as you draw up your questions, which ones are goin
sent a challenge to the interviewee, and which ones will be more wel
Generally speaking, more challenging questions will be those that th
wee may not wish to discuss, perhaps because the subject matter is to

```

```
t allow the source to cast herself in a favourable light, or which seek
tablished and customary position, will be less threatening. You will ther
e want to consider carefully how to order your questions. Julian Sher,
ard-winning investigative journalist and author, divides interview conten
o three basic types.

 Some interviews may be only or mainly information (witnesses to a
 fire or car crash, for example), some might be emotion (a tearful family
 reunion with a returning soldier or hostage), and others might be
 accountability (a government minister). The best interviews have all
 three. And good interviews proceed in that order from information to
 emotion to accountability. It’s pretty hard to start with a “beat-up” ques tion and then go back and ask for information. The key thing is that you
 have to determine ahead of time—and prepare your questions accord ingly—what mix of information, emotion, and accountability you want.

 You also need to consider which questions will yield dry information, an
ich others will yield rich, human anecdotes. Intensely personal questions a
 more likely to produce anecdotes, but they may also be sensitive, dependin
 the comfort level of the interviewee in discussing them. Someone who h
scussed a personal matter many times will be far more willing to discuss
ain than someone who has never been asked about it before. In the latt
e, you will want to ease into the discussion gently. On the other hand,
meone has discussed something many times, he or she may have developed
e way of discussing it, and follow up questions may be needed to search f
ling details.
 Obviously, the mix of question types will hinge somewhat on who you a
terviewing and why. An interview with a person who suffered radiation po
ning after a childhood spent playing on a nuclear waste dump, for instanc
y be driven primarily by questions that explore personal experience. O
e other hand, an interview with a political figure about a new policy to cra
wn on irresponsible nuclear-waste handlers may be driven primarily
ctual questions.
 The form of the question is just as important. The overriding message he
st be that old saw, “Keep it simple, stupid.” Writer Sally Adams put it partic
ly well: “Since the first interview requirement is to communicate, commo
nse dictates that short questions are better than long ones, simple questio
tter than convoluted ones, clear questions better than abstruse ones.”5
 We must also pay homage to the groundbreaking work of investig
e writer John Sawatsky. The author of two bestsellers on Canada’s s

```

```
roney, Sawatsky is one of the few people who has actually studied t
ds of interviews to assess what kinds of questions work most of the
 which ones fail. His basic message is that interviewers should stay in
calls “input” mode, asking simple, evocative, open-ended questions
ourage interview subjects to describe and explain, instead of asking ag
, closed-ended questions that require only that the subject confirm or d
 Sawatsky argues that closed questions, such as, “Were you surprised?
e you a racist?”, while sometimes sounding informed and tough, allow i
wees to answer simply yes or no, then stitch on whatever information
ose. “Socially, people are taught to add a postscript to a confirmation
al,” says Sawatsky. “As journalists, we hope the ‘p.s. will describe or ex
 issue we've raised. That's interviewing by accident. If you get somebody
sn't want to play, you're in trouble.”®
 He also counsels against

 ¢ long-winded questions weighed down by unnecessary or lea
   statements (“How should people react to that? I’ve heard many pe
   are angry. );
 * questions that betray the opinion of the interviewer (“Isn't it true
   your company has poisoned thousands of children?”);
 ¢ multiple questions that allow an interviewee to choose one or
   other to answer (“Why did you do that? Didn't you consider the
   sequences?”); and
 ¢ questions that include what Sawatsky calls “trigger words,” w
   prompt the respondent to address the word rather than the q
   (“Why did you do such a reprehensible thing?” Likely answer: “We
   not reprehensible at all. In fact, our nuclear by-products managem
   program is second to none.”)’

 Of course, closed-ended questions can help you to confirm basic facts
d you attend the meeting?” or “You were born in Vancouver, correc
n-ended questions on the other hand, force people to think. “Genui
gh questions compel people to go further than they otherwise wou
atsky says. Examples are “Why did you do that?’, “How did that happe
at did you see?”, or “What went through your mind?” While soun
er, these actually make it much more difficult for an experienced su

sage track, fully loaded with talking points.
 Once more, and at the risk of being repetitive: A thought-out list of q
```

```
here to do it

r the most part, this is usually a choice between using a telephone and meet
g the person face to face.
  In your investigative work, telephone interviews and emails will play
portant role, especially for the many foundational interviews that develo
ur knowledge of a subject. Generally speaking, interviews that seek
ther facts and context are more easily done by telephone, or via an Interne
deo-conferencing service, such as Skype or Google Hangouts, than those th
ek personal details or insights. This is because it is difficult to develop a pe

nal rapport using voice/video contact alone. Also, confrontational interviews
ich often necessitate more difficult questions, are usually more challengin

 The most important interviews should be done in person. You can mak
e contact and read body language; it is a more human relationship. The be
cation may be in the person's natural environment, such as an office or home
ich may offer evidence of personal or professional interests in the for
 mementos, certificates, family pictures, and so on. Often, discussing suc
jects can provide the perfect icebreaker. If you have no choice but to spe

 A variant of the in-person interview is one done at the “scene of t
ime.” If your investigation involves an incident with a specific locale, or
ere is a regular spot where the person you are interviewing was involved
e story—an example might be a busker on a particular street corner—by
ans arrange to do the interview there. Being at a location can provide yo
th scenes to describe, and prompt useful questions, as well as help the inte
ewee to remember experiences more acutely. Steve Buttry, director of tailor
ograms at the American Press Institute, suggests trying a “moving” interview

k her to show you the house and the yard.”
  If you are approaching people in public places—as Kines did when workin

thout a notebook or recording device, and begin with casual conversatio
fore moving into interview mode. You should still identify yourself quick
nless you're working undercover, of course), but you may decide not to ta
 es. Instead, you can jot down your recollections of the conversation short
```

```
 to act

unds obvious to say that a good interviewer needs to listen well, but

ng sounds or gestures of approval. A good listener is active, not passive.
u bury yourself in notes, fidget, look out the window, yawn, slouch,

yllabic answers.
```

```
ep a low profile
ing your interviews in the correct sequence is just as important as organi
g the flow within each interview. In our hypothetical example of Wasteglow’
clear-waste dumping, you wouldn't approach Wasteglow first. Likely, you
t until most of your other interviews were completed. Generally, investig
s work from the outside in, advancing through a series of concentric rin
il they reach the target of the investigation, at the very centre.
 The outside ring might consist of complainants, such as the radiation vi
. From there, you might go to generic expert sources, people who are famili
th the problem at a high level, but who are not involved with the specific
u might proceed to people closer to the responsibility, such as the publ
icials in charge of overseeing nuclear-waste disposal. Finally, you would rea
 target. You should limit, as much as possible, the chance that word of yo
ivities will reach the centre while you work through the outer rings. The mor
rning the target has, the easier it is to develop a public-relations strategy
unter your research, or to try to squelch contacts who might speak to you.
 By starting with people who are antagonistic to the target, moving throug
ople who are essentially neutral, and on to people who may be closer to t
get, you improve your chances of working undetected for the longest peri
time.
 Some might feel uncomfortable about this technique, that it is somethin
in to an ambush. Really, though, it is simply a matter of keeping your inve
ation confidential in order to avoid contaminating the story by influencin
rself, much as a scientist avoids having an effect on an experiment aimed
ting a hypothesis.

parently bored? Does the person assume a position of power behind a des
invite you to sit casually on a couch? What’s in the room? Is there a shelf f
books? If so, what books?
 Such observations provoke additional questions and provide you with t
```

```
The Dilemma of “Off the Record” ;

 etermining what your subject means and wants

ooner or later, you will hear an interviewee say “This is off the record.” It is
f the most overused and misunderstood phrases in journalism.
  Traditionally, “off the record” meant you could not use the information
 l, except as a starting point to develop questions aimed at extracting the sa
nformation from others who spoke “on the record.” In our experience, mo
eople really mean, “Don't use my name.” This is more properly called not
ttribution or going on background. If someone asks for their comments to
ff the record, politely inquire what they mean.
  You commonly see unnamed sources used in political stories, but less of
n investigative work. Unquoted sources may well be used in investigations
rovide hints, leads, and advice, but they don’t appear in print that frequent
his is because an investigative story needs to rely on what is established a
herefore, on the record. This said, it is extremely important to understand
ifferent types of attribution, what they mean, and the implications they ca
oth for reporters and sources.

 n the record
f your contact is on the record, then you can quote the source by name and
he information provided without restriction. If possible, this is where you w
 be all the time. The audience knows who is speaking, and the speaker ta

```

```
means you cannot quote the source by name, but you can fully use t
ation and in most cases describe the person generally, using a euphem
ke “a government insider.” Depending on your understanding with t
, you may be able to run direct quotations. A promise of confidentiality
d, although this should be negotiated at the time of the interview. As w
elow, however, be extremely careful about undertaking such agreements.

ckground
nformation may be used, but is generally not to be attributed to anyone
uoted directly. A promise of confidentiality is implied. This is a stanc
nly assumed by government sources.

ep background
typically means the information may not be used—and certainly no
d directly—in a story. It can, however, be used to corroborate other infor
, and can be the basis for questions put to others who may be able
e the same information on the record. A promise of confidentiality
med.

e record
ntioned, this theoretically means the same as “on deep background”;
e, however, it can mean almost anything, so you need to clarify it wit
rson.

identiality

 do promise confidentiality, journalistic ethics bind you not to reveal t
of the source, ever, or until such time as the source releases you from t

stances, because Canadian law does not give journalists an absolute rig

can states!® do not exist in Canada, and a federal private-members’ bi

 few Canadian journalists have been jailed for protecting a source,” judge
 on a case-by-case basis whether a source is worthy of protection.
```

```
urt may be willing to allow a journalist to protect a source. Andrew McInto
```

```
—who helped him investigate the notorious federal sponsorship program,
nown as AdScam. A judicial inquiry credited Leblanc’s diligent reportin

Judges must take into account “the high societal interest in investigativ

ital to the integrity of the administration of justice.” Lawyers for The Glob

n the third case, an Ontario judge found Hamilton Spectator reporter Ken

n light of these rulings, here are some suggestions for dealing with
s request to remain confidential:

  Avoid using unnamed sources. The public has a right to know whos
  behind the information and allegations that the media dissemin  ate; this makes sources accountable and lends credibility to what
  reported. Many news organizations require an editor or producer t
  approve the use of unnamed sources and may require at least on

```

```
    Journalists’s (CAJ) Statement of Principles for Investigative Journali
    declares that journalists should promise confidentiality only when
    source’s co-operation “puts them at risk of harm or personal hardsh
    (i.e., a whistle-blower who might lose his/her job, or a mole with
    organized crime).””’
    Clarify what the source is seeking. Is it a matter of attributing quot
    and information to an unnamed source? Does the source expect his
    her identity will never be revealed? Or something in between?
    Never promise complete anonymity. Journalists are obliged, und
    defamation laws, to disclose a source’s identity to an editor or prod
    cer, who has a duty to verify the information a reporter gathers befo
    it is published or broadcast.*°
    Level with the source and negotiate an exit strategy. Journalists shou
    warn sources that it is impossible, under Canadian law, to offer
    ironclad promise of confidentiality. A journalist can promise,
    instance, not to reveal the source’s identity (other than to a dir
    supervisor) unless ordered to do so by a court. Or the source m
    agree that, if the journalist ever faces a fine or jail time for refusing
    testify, he or she will come forward at that point.
    Insist on the fine print. “If you are willing to go to jail to pr
    tect a source, say so,’ the CAJ advises in its Statement of Principl
    “Otherwise, spell out the conditions.” Whatever promise is mad
    journalists must ensure that they can follow through and that th
    employer is willing, if necessary, to absorb the legal costs of defendi
    a promise of confidentiality. Journalists also should heed the less
    of the Maher Arar case, where unnamed government sources used
    media to smear Arar, a Canadian detained and tortured in Syria, a
    terrorist.*! The CAJ believes sources should be on notice that, if t
    lie or knowingly provide false information, all deals are off, and
    journalist is free to expose them.
```

```
king with Media-Relations People

was a time when journalists could reach most sources directly, or through
onal secretary or receptionist. However, an enormous media-relations
ry has sprung up and grown dramatically in Canada over the past several
. Its machinery is increasingly sophisticated and intrusive. Media-relations
 are very likely to be involved when you call any sizable private company
ic institution. Their primary job is often to ensure that nothing is said that
 fit a preordained organization line. Even if you get to talk to the people i
 or to mid-level functionaries, the media-relations officers will be involved
nging the interview, and may insist on sitting in on your discussion.
f course, journalists will always develop and nurture sources within
ations, but when it comes to official public contact, the media-relations
will usually intervene. Many employees of private companies and public
 are under strict orders to refer any media calls to the press office, someon pain of dismissal.
u might as well develop the best relationship you can with these people.
are former journalists who have discovered better pay and working con in public relations. Preparation is, as ever, the best tactic. Try to know as
as you can going in, with hopes of recognizing subterfuge and misinformaen you see it. Treat media-relations staff with the same respect and courtesy
uld anyone else, but be insistent on having your questions answered; make
 that if a source refuses to answer questions, your story will say so.
 valuable reason for staying on good terms with media-relations officers
 they are often the gatekeepers who control access to the people with

```

```
bject matter, you can turn many press officers into allies. At the very le
u can avoid being placed on their mental blacklist of rude reporters.
 When it comes to the key accountability interviews, you must speak to
ople who are truly accountable. Media officers will try to insist on answer
ur questions themselves. Make no mistake; this is a power struggle.
dia person wants to keep the accountable person out of the spotlight, a
om the heat, so to speak. You need the accountable official because reade
ewers, and listeners arent stupid. They know the difference in heft betwe
e public-relations officer and the head honcho.
 In this scenario, you start softly and shift toward a harder position u
u land the interview.
 You can begin by politely explaining that you need to put your questi
 the person in charge in order that the company or public body’s posit
rry the most weight with the audience. If that doesn’t do the job, be mo
sistent. In many cases, the media-relations person will be bluffing, in
pe you accept him or her as the source. That, after all, is what this per
paid to do. You may need to tell the media person categorically that only
terview with the responsible official will be acceptable and that you will ma
clear in the story that the truly accountable person refused to speak if tha
e case. Often, the realization that you are willing to play hardball will t
e tide.
 You shouldn't accept an accountability interview with a media-relati
rson—or canned emailed responses—without putting up a vigorous fight.

 Final Note on Media Training

 Chapter 1, we mentioned media trainer George Merlis, who counsels
ients to develop intentional message statements (IMSs) that they can d
to interviews to ensure the company line is delivered and to pay “factual
rvice” to questions before “bridging” to one of the message tracks. We
scussed the McLoughlin Wedge’, a method to introduce pre-planned cont
roughout an interview.
 George Merlis and Barry McLoughlin are among a growing army
nsultants, many of them former reporters, who train senior politicia
reaucrats, and company officials to deftly handle interviews. They put cli
rough simulated interviews, and teach them how to take control by watch
r the “exit ramps” that will allow them to move to their canned messag
ke no mistake, when you face one of these trained people, you are deal
th a real adversary, and one who has been trained to think of you in exa
ose terms. Among the favourite tricks of trained interviewees are

```

```
  refusing to answer on the basis of “privacy, security, or budget
  secrecy;” and
  having the final word by recapping the canned message at the end o
  the interview.*®

ecause Canada is a free country, we can't stop interview subjects from
ng and practising these methods. But there are simple things you can do
nter their wall of training. The most basic is to be persistent—if a source
t answer a question, or “bridges” to other territory, politely bridge back
sk your question again. Ultimately, the person isn't obliged to answer i
u want to have that refusal to answer on the record. Investigative reporeed to be alert and aware of these stonewalling techniques so as not t
control of the interview to the subject. For this, of course, you need t
closely and focus on the information you need, so that you know if your
on was answered satisfactorily in the first place.
se silences. Remember John Sawatsky’s advice on open-ended questions.
, just as the media trainers have studied the media, we should study
dia trainers. Borrow Merlis’s book from the library, and buy a copy o
ghlin’s media strategies guide.

rviewing step by step

  Identify the appropriate source.
  Be prepared. Do your research.
  Prepare questions based on your research.
  Decide on a location—in person is best.
  Have a strategy for the interview.
 — If possible, keep all interviews on the record.
  Record the interview if possible.
  Transcribe the results for important interviews.
```

```
 from a $1.2 billion profit to loss of almost $5.9 billion. Or, stated more
cally, during that period, the company lost more than $7 billion. Still
ere silver linings. The company still had lots of cash and assets, tha
 quickly turn into cash, of almost $1.6 billion in 2014, only slightl
n the two previous years. And it was still spending money—though
hing amount—on research and development, a possible sign that th
y still had lots of fight left. We will examine these numbers in more
uring our breakdown of the company’s 2014 annual report.
 let’s continue for a bit longer with the narrative, because stories about
, if written properly, can be absorbing tales of decisions—good and
pectations—deflated or inflated—and deceit or, in BlackBerry’s case
ulation.
cording to the many accounts written about the company, BlackBerry

es, would never abandon its secure and keyboard-equipped mobile

ogle were grabbing larger portions of the market. How? By appealing t
rs who wanted smartphones for a range of activities in addition to tex

’s falling fortunes, “Investors who have lived through the destruction
```

```
kills Exercises

 Use the bankruptcy statistics from the Office of the Superintendent
 Bankruptcy Canada (www.ic.gc.ca/eic/site/bsf-osb.nsf/eng/h_br0101
 html) to monitor business and consumer insolvencies, as Teresa Wrig
 from The Guardian did with her story entitled, “Number of Islanders fi
  for bankruptcy takes dramatic rise: Numbers up by almost 37 per c
  in a year’?
 Find a company in your area that is facing insolvency. Follow the develo
 ments, and read the documents for stories that other people aren't tell
  or that the company is avoiding.
 Choose a publicly traded company that is also a major employer in yo
 market, and use proxy statements to track what their executives ha
 been making during a three-to-five-year period. You'll have to dump
 numbers into a spreadsheet, and do the math yourself. Once you've co
 pleted the calculations, contrast the executives’ wage hikes with the sal
  increases of the company’s front-line workers. If there's a large dispar
  that has gone unnoticed, then you have a good story. Increasingly, this
 what newspapers like The New York Times are doing.**
 Perform the same task, this time with executives who work for the pu
  lic sector in your province or territory. In jurisdictions like Ontario a
 Alberta, governments post salaries of bureaucrats who make more th
 $100,000 a year.** Included in these databases are large salaries of CE
 of hospitals and Crown corporations, who may have trouble balanci
 budgets and giving their employees decent raises but are receiving han
 some salaries and yearly wage increases nonetheless.
  Use some basic ratios—profit margin, operating margin, return on equi
  return on assets, price-to-earnings ratio—to analyze a publicly trad
 company for a three-to-five-year period.** For definitions of these rati
 please see the appendix. In his book Understanding Financial Statement
  Jay Taparia writes, “Entering the figures into a spreadsheet will help y
  learn the numbers. Not only will you start to understand the business, y
  will also start to see trends (and maybe even some parts of the story).”*”
```

```
      Getting Behind
      Closed Doors

       Using the Information Laws

Some of the most important knowledge we have about how we are
 overned has come from disclosures under access-to-information
nd freedom-of-information laws.

roduction

ower of government documents lies in their authority. They are primar
s, windows into the minds of those who act in our name, incontestabl
nce of both intent and execution. Their authorship is clear and their con
accountable.
s such, they are the subject of vigorous pursuit for journalists like Ji
ill. “These materials help illuminate public policy and decision-making
cted officials in an important way because they are created by the govern themselves—not by partisan opponents or distant observers,” he says.
mong the illuminating stories that Bronskill has wrestled onto the publi
a over the past two decades through access to information requests wa
 series that pulled back the curtain on the government’s so-called tor
emos. Most Canadians might have believed its government’s position o
 intelligence gathered through torture was crystal clear: We don't.
 federal inquiry called after the torture of Canadian Maher Arar—an
ed, year-long imprisonment in Damascus aided by flawed information
ed by the RCMP—issued clear directions to government “aimed at elim
g any possible Canadian complicity in torture, avoiding the risk of othe
 rights abuses and ensuring accountability.”' The government appeared
e agreed.
ut in 2010, Bronskill twigged to a potential softening of that stance i
 federal directive mentioned in a report from the Canadian Securit
igence Service watchdog. He filed a formal access-to-information request
 document. When it arrived, it triggered an agenda-setting series beginwith a February 2012 story that reported, “The federal government ha
ed Canada’s spy agency to use information that may have been extracted

```

```
  That order, Bronskill reported, represented a previously unknown “rever
 policy for the Conservative government, which once insisted the Canadi
curity Intelligence Service would discard information if there was any inkl
might be tainted.”
  Follow-up stories, also based on records ohaeiea through informati
quests, exposed a more detailed directive to CSIS, its interpretation wit
e spy agency, and how the RCMP and the federal border agency were hand
milar directives to accept torture-tainted information.
  “These later directives outlined the process for deciding whether to sh
nformation when there is a ‘substantial risk that doing so might result
omeone in custody being abused,” says Bronskill.*
  The stories triggered a firestorm of debate in Parliament, brought hea
ords from human rights groups and the United Nations Committee Agai
rture, and inspired a flurry of discussion across the country—discussion
uldn't have happened otherwise.

  Federal access-to-information (ATI) and provincial freedomnformation (FOI) legislation is our most forceful legal expression of the publi
ght to know. These laws give citizens the legal right to request records held
overnments at all levels. They offer journalists, researchers, and any Canadi
tizen the ability to look beyond the public record to learn more about hidd
overnment actions, policies, and decisions.
  “The overarching purpose of access to information legislation is to fac
te democracy,’ according to a 1997 judgment by former Supreme Court
anada justice Gérard La Forest. “It does so in two related ways. It helps
nsure first, that citizens have the information required to participate meanin
lly in the democratic process, and secondly, that politicians and bureaucr
emain accountable to the citizenry.”®
  Access-to-information and freedom-of-information laws have long b
sed by journalists, politicians, citizens, community groups, researchers,
ompanies to obtain numerous public records, including data, corresponden
iefing notes, and memos. For the investigative journalist, access requests
basic research tool. The legislation opens the door for in-depth research
e machinations of government, the reasons why decisions are made,
eir impact on the public. If used strategically, information laws can help
iscover how taxpayer dollars are spent, warn us of public health risks, unco
orruption, and hold elected officials to account.
  Some of the most important knowledge we have about how we
overned has come from disclosures under access-to-information
reedom-of-information laws. These disclosures have helped inform us ab
e troubling conduct of Canadian soldiers in Somalia and police officers

```

```
w about the neglect of elderly people in nursing homes and the abuse o
en in daycares. And they have brought to our attention health threats lik
orne illnesses, safety inspection oversights, and potentially fatal adverse
eactions.
eally, there would be no need for legislation dictating formal procedor requesting and accessing public records. Governments would release
ation of this nature upon request as a matter of routine in the service o
blic interest. But human and political instincts dictate otherwise. And
ation laws, while valuable, are by no means an assurance that important
ation will be made public. Any routine user of information laws knows
 can be extremely difficult to obtain public records if governments don’t
o release them.
here are growing concerns about the accessibility of public information
ada, especially among journalists. Canadians do not read, see, or hear
 as many access-to-information stories as fill US newspapers and news
, largely because Canadian journalists cannot access records that are
ely available in the States. Many fear the regulatory regime is getting
singly restrictive.
 2012 international ranking of nations based on their freedom-ofation regimes placed Canada fifty-first out of 89 countries, behind
s including Angola, Colombia, Serbia, and Slovenia.®
s a country that was once among the world’s leaders in government
ss, it is unfortunate that Canada has dropped so far down the list,” the
s authors concluded.
ountless volumes of public information in Canada are locked in govt filing cabinets, tightly guarded by eagle-eyed bureaucrats. Often, i
months, even years, to access information that should be provided within
n addition, high fees for the records put them beyond the reach of many
ers. And even when records are released, the contents can be “sevor blacked out—thus rendering documents meaningless.
 philosophical bent toward self-protection and secrecy dominates many
nment offices across Canada. And while freedom-of-information laws
a counter to those human tendencies, their efficacy is highly limited and
s subject to the whims of bureaucrats. It is the product of what has been
d a “culture of secrecy” within government.
he integrity of the federal access-to-information program is at serious
Canada’s information commissioner Suzanne Legault wrote in her 2013
 report, citing a “lack of leadership and resources” that has created long
 and even non-responses to requests. “Access to information is funda to Canada’s system of government, a key tool that facilitates citizen
```

```
nly is Canadians’ participation in government thwarted but ultimately,
alth of Canadian democracy is at stake.””
  A core issue behind the failure in public accountability, Legault conclud

nternational levels.”
  The litany of offences cited is long.

as so understaffed in its handling of formal requests “that it was unable

hould have,’ the report says.
```

```
  Evolving digital communications are also posing a threat to governme

ansparency.

 What all of this means—the culture of secrecy, outdated and restrict
gislation, along with steadily disappearing records—is that requests for what
ten clearly public information can be routinely denied or delayed far beyo
ason. It means Canadians know far less than they should about governme
tions and their implications.
  Despite those challenges, there are ways to access important public r
ds, to learn the system, and to challenge unreasonable delays and denia

he Laws

ne might assume that legislation designed to provide access to public recor
ould be heavily used by journalists. But a relatively small percentage of repo
rs actually file requests as a matter of routine—evidence of how cumberso
nd slow the process can be.
  Usually, ATI and FOI requests are not useful for reporters working on ti
adlines. It's not generally a tool for breaking news stories. It is a method
ursuing issues that are likely to be as important in a month or two as they
w, stories that go beyond the press release or official comment of the d
nd it’s a way of pursuing issues that aren't part of the public debate at all,
ould be.
  Because this is a practical research guide, we will not dwell too long
gal details of the various information laws across the country. It is importan
owever, to understand the basic legal foundations upon which citizens
quests for information to governments. The general principles underlyi

```

```
government-held documents, to detail the limits or “exemptions” to tha
and to establish an appeal procedure if access is denied. The laws express
mocratic belief that governments must be open and accountable.
 Canada, we have a federal Access to Information Act as well as separate
ial and territorial freedom-of-information laws. The fundamental utilit
e is similar. They generally impose a 30-day deadline for government
ments to respond to written requests for information; the departments
owever, request extensions. The laws outline fees for access, including
r receiving filed requests, compiling the records, and providing copies
uments. They list the agencies covered by the legislation and the specifi
 that are exempt.
e various laws differ in their specific exemptions to access, the relativ
veness of the appeals processes, and the fees imposed. Delays in accesscords can also vary widely among government departments across th

.

ptions

ted exemptions to access often present the most serious challenge t
ng government records. All information laws in Canada detail specifi
 that cannot be released. These might include internal government
ations, business secrets, documents that could breach solicitor—client
ge, records containing personal information, cabinet confidences,
forcement and national-security records, and documents covering
—provincial relations. Some of those exemptions are considered “discre’ officials are not required to apply them.
s not uncommon to receive hundreds of pages of records from an
quest only to find the vast majority of words blacked out in response
ific legislated exemptions. Variation also exists in how wide a net th
t laws cast over the institutions they cover. For example, some provfreedom-of-information laws apply to universities and professional
gulatory bodies, but in much of the country, those institutions—which
ut vital public interest work—are beyond the reach of legislated public

.
stitutions or specific documents excluded by the information laws are
nition, outside the scope of public access. Requesters have virtually no
e in these circumstances. Although they can appeal a request denial,

etation, however, and when it comes to information laws, those interons can play a vital role in determining whether a record should be made

```

```
xemptions and limitations to the widest possible extent in order to arg
ainst release of records. Rather than presuming a right of access, experienc
esearchers in Canada often brace for the bureaucratic default position of “N
hen filing sensitive information requests.
 “When you're asking for information, you're going against the natu
ureaucratic tendency to be cautious,”'® says Alasdair Roberts, a leadi
anadian scholar on information laws.
  Clear restrictions in the legislation need to be fairly and judicious
bserved, but many requesters and experts argue that exemptions are applied
```
