til there is a complaint,” former information commissioner John Reid wrot
Too often, departments have been content to address only the question: ‘M
e requested records be kept secret?’”!”

ees
                                                                                                .

ees for accessing government records in Canada vary from province to pr
ce. The three main fees are an initial application fee, the cost of retrieving
ecords, and the cost of appealing information-request decisions.
  The initial fee to file a request is $5 at the federal level and in some pr
ces. Other provinces do not charge for this. The highest fee is in Alber
here requesters are charged $25 up front.
  By far the most onerous fees associated with information requests are th
harged for researching, retrieving, and vetting the records. Once a governme
epartment researches your request and locates the records, it will estim
he cost of researching and preparing them. It may assign no fee if the requ
as precise and specific to a single record or two. Large requests, asking
undreds of pages of documents or requiring computer work to prepare a g
rnment database for release, can cause fee estimates to balloon to thousan
f dollars.
  Federally, your $5 request buys you a few hours of research time before
tra fees apply. There is similar free time in almost every province; Ontar
 the exception. So, dividing large requests into several smaller requests he
aximize the bonus search time you receive. New Brunswick is alone amo
risdictions in that it has abolished all fees, so requests there are always free
  Requesters generally have the right to request fee waivers for retrieval
hotocopying based on either an inability to pay for the records or becau
eleasing the information would serve the public interest. This requires a wr
n appeal outlining the reasons. While journalistic requests are clearly ma

```

```
 public interest—as opposed to personal or business requests that serve
 interests—government officials rarely award the fee waivers without
nal compelling arguments.
e third major fee, related to appeal submissions, also varies. Some prov et you file appeals free of charge, but Ontario, for example, charges $25
 an appeal to the provincial information and privacy commissioner. The
g menu of fees in different jurisdictions can produce very different finan
rdens for requesters. High fees represent a significant impediment t
 because, while major media outlets may be able to afford hundreds o
nds of dollars for records of vital importance, smaller outlets or individay not. For the public, those financial restrictions can spell the difference
n learning about matters of importance and remaining in the dark.
ome government officials argue that administering information laws
ive, requiring rising fees in order to ensure “cost recovery.’ It’s a position
y challenged by access advocates who call access to public information
s fundamental as voting.

ys

gislation may impose a 20- or 30-day deadline by which government
 are to be provided to requesters, but that deadline frequently comes and
ithout disclosure. Requesters have waited many months, even years, fo
 to be released.
ile straightforward requests for simple reports or studies are often ful
ithin the statutory time limit, more complex requests dealing with larg
rs of records or politically sensitive documents generally take much
 Many journalists tell stories of information requests that dragged on
eyond reasonable time frames, sometimes reaching into years.
 sweeping 2008 study of global information laws by Canadian journalist
 Tromp found Canada’s federal law fails to meet international standards
key points set out by a London-based human-rights organization and
ed by the United Nations Special Rapporteur on Freedom of Opinion

How to Make Your Request

 esearching

 e first step in obtaining government records is not filing an informati

  Appeals are directed to one of two offices depending on the level of
rnment youre dealing with. For federal requests, you approach the fed
nformation commission; provincial/territorial or municipal appeals
andled by access and privacy officials in each province and territory. M
anadian jurisdictions have an appeals process in which ombudsmen or adj
ators consider submissions from both sides before issuing a decision based
he legislation. A mediator may first be assigned to attempt an informal res
ion to the dispute.
  In some cases, appeal rulings are binding. Ontario's information and
acy commissioner, for example, has the power to compel the release of reco
eemed to be public. The federal information commissioner and some prov
al appeal bodies, however, don’t have this power. Even if they rule in fa
f the requester, the final decision regarding release of the records rema
ith government officials. Generally speaking, the moral suasion of an ap
ecision is sufficient. If not, the appeal body may sometimes take the case
ourt on behalf of the requester. Likewise, requesters who are unsatisfied
he findings of an appeal body can take their cases to court on their own.
  Remember our earlier caution: appealing at the fee-estimate stage co

lic interest, considering the media’s ability to use such information t

o know matters of vital importance. That argument is, of course, sub

ed according to the nature of the specific information and the potentia
nterest benefits. But appeals bodies across the country have accepted
gument and released exempted information in cases where the records

The Challenges

espite holding a legally protected right to access public records, many in

 eed to pose their questions properly, pursue the progress of requests w
 ficials, and be vigorous about appealing decisions that fall short of statu
equirements. Records containing potentially sensitive or embarrassing in

roblems with access.
  “Government departments are increasingly sophisticated in identify
olitically sensitive requests, and most major departments will have proc
res for dealing with communications implications of sensitive requests,”
lasdair Roberts. “If you're making a politically sensitive request, your req
ill be delayed. You should also assume that communications advisors in
rnment are looking at your request and developing ‘communications produc
o help the department deal with the fallout from your request.”*4
  “Communications products” is a bureaucratic phrase that refers to ad
or ministers and spokespeople on how to respond to public questions ra
  a result of an information request once the information is publis
 overnment department staffers also routinely prepare notes for ministers
 ow to address questions in the legislature. Keep in mind that those rec
re also subject to information legislation, meaning that individuals who
o know how their request was handled behind the scenes can file sepa
equests for the material. The records should show when the request
eceived, the office that held the records, whether there were consultations
 ommunications staff and ministerial advisors, and the back-and-forth c
 unications between officials on the process of handling the request.

 ic—private partnerships

rn Canadian governments have become ubiquitous brides, anxious
  into partnerships with private industry for the purposes of deliveri

rnments looking to do more—or as much—with less. Private indust

What You Can Get

his brings us to a brief survey of the types of records commonly sought
successfully accessed) under ATI and FOI laws. It cannot be an exhaustive li

Expense records

~
Among the most high-profile documents i informat
requests are expense records submitted by public officials. These form the
or one of the classic watchdog roles of the press: keeping an eye on how pu
money is spent. This material can contain startling revelations about how
payers finance meals, travel, and other expenses for officials operating wit
proper accountability. Such cases have been documented at all levels of gov
ment across the country.
  As we discussed earlier, federal disclosure rules now make it mandatory
ministries to post travel and hospitality spending on their websites. But o
expense records remain within the domain of formal information reque
And routine disclosure of travel and hospitality expenses is not always cust
ary at other levels of government.
  In 2009, Calgary Herald reporters used provincial freedom-of-informat
legislation to access expense records showing the city’s mayor spent $28,07
taxpayer dollars on a one-day speaker's event for top city bureaucrats. Incl
in the expenses were “$1863 for a general manager's office chair, $390 for ma
zines in one department, and thousands of dollars for managers’ meals
hotel stays,” the Herald reported.*!
  In 2013, Toronto Sun journalist Sue-Ann Levy used freedom of info
tion to detail how executives with Toronto's 2015 Pan Am Games “regu
expensed what should have been hospitality dinners with each other
unnamed guests and called them business meals.”

```

```
  Expense claims for meals, taxis and parking were submitted witho
  ts or approvals but paid—even though the claimant did not submit
  en explanation’ as to why the backup was missing and the approver
  ocument why the expense was paid.” The 2015 Pan Am CEO Ian Troop w
  ced two months later.

  he most high-profile journalistic investigation into police conduct

   The series relied on Toronto police data, obtained through freedom-o
  mation requests, to report a range of ethnic disparities in police treatme
  izens beginning with the simple but powerful conclusion that a bla
  n arrested for simple drug possession was twice as likely as a white pers
  held for a bail hearing.*°
  hat analysis was based on six years of police arrest records contained i

   e debate about police actions in Toronto and across the country, and
  2.7 billion class-action libel suit by the police against the Star, alleg
   ries portrayed all Toronto police officers as racist. (Three courts rul
  sively that the suit could not proceed because the series could not
  onably understood” to imply that every officer was racist.**)
   joint Toronto Star-Miami Herald investigation in 2013 into Canadian
  s who exploit children in foreign countries thanks to gaping loophol
   law was aided by a key RCMP document obtained through an access-t
  mation request.
  Filing for access to information records is often a frustrating waste

a stunning finding that reverberated across the country: 145 childre
d while in the care of the Alberta government between 1999 and 2013—
triple the number reported by the province.*

ges of information. Kleiss argued for—and won—a fee waiver by argu

icials had not only blacked out the name of the child, but also the

 . Officials also blacked out the name of the child welfare authors and third-party agencies that had cared for the child, as well as

es, even recommendations that resulted from internal reviews
e redacted.

han 3000 death records to be released for the 14-year time frame. B

 Among the most important responsibilities of governments is the mainte
ce of public safety and security. Governments at all levels monitor industr
d public services through regular vigilance, reporting, and inspections.
ray of records in this area is wide and far-reaching.
 But the instinctive assumption here is that governments of all stri
 Canada are accountable for the safety of most areas of human endeavo
e question is, which government agency and how does its oversight sys
nction?
 Inspection records are among the most compelling public records availa

 researchers interested in how a particular government system operates
w well it protects the public interest. They gen i insights
vernment ins 0 monitor everything from water quality to pipeli
 food safety. Generally, inspectors make such visits on a regular schedu
e results of those inspections tend to exist both on paper and in governme
tabases. Working with inspection records often requires a three-step proce

nderstand how the inspection system works
nsider the kinds of questions that might help you understand how thi
rk in the field you're researching and what documents or data might exis
lp you. If it’s an area of government regulation, how do officials exercise t
gulatory authority? What's the process by which public officials docume
eir actions? Perhaps the most basic questi any public official re
r protecting the public interest is simply this: “When something goes wro
here-do-you write it down?” Answers to that question will often provid
arting point that will help you understand where to go next.
  Inspection records are one of the places where proble i
e authors of those reports are government officials who are presuma
biased, informed, and acting in the public interest. But it’s important
derstand how a particular inspection system works. For example, how o
 officials conduct their inspections? Are there mandatory time frames?
ey call the company ahead of time or make surprise visits? What exactly
ey look for? Where do they record what they see? What are the names
ose records? What kind of information is in them? When inspectors get b
 the office after one of these inspections, what do they do with their findin

```

```
database? If so, what's the database called? Is it public? Just as importt's missing? How reliable are the records? Do the inspection result
 specific designations? Can they trigger charges? Under which piece o
ion?
 of those questions will help you build a clear picture of how governtackle specific areas of public concern, and will help you graduall
and how well they might be accomplishing that goal.

 Requests

   all kinds—whether reports and studies on paper or vast inspectio

ta journalism, discussed in Chapter 11, is an increasingly powerful an

```
 “While doing preliminary research before an FOI request is always wort
le, it’s essential for FOI requests for data,” he says. “You need to know how
ta you're requesting is gathered and stored in order to ask for it effectively.
 Record format is also a far more important issue for consideration in
e of data requests.
 Many government departments release records as PDF files that are dif
t to analyze or get into spreadsheet formats. To save time and annoyanc
'll want to do your due diligence on the data, how they’re structured,
rmat, and the fields of information inside. It will help you ask for the recor

formation that will hold up the process.
 One Skelton request to the federal Correctional Service sought the nu
r of Canadians on parole in each municipality in Canada. Note the det
ntained in the request based on Skelton’s research of the database: “It is
derstanding that the information required to compile these figures should

y privacy concerns, I am specifically not asking for any identifiable inform
n about each offender such as their name, year of birth, type of offence,
am simply requesting statistical information on how many parolees reside
ch Canadian municipality.’

rd electronic database format (i.e., Microsoft Access, Microsoft Excel, or C
e), with a single row for each municipality with the number of parolees
 adjoining row. Exporting such a file should be relatively simple with
andard database program.”
 When government pushback comes, Skelton advises speaking with
ta experts inside the ministry or department to try and clear it directly rat
an working through a communications mediator.

rking department's data coordinator.

ess rate was about one story for every 10 requests.
th data requests, the success ratio is the reverse: about 90 per cent,

lusion

k often seems stacked against those seeking public information. While
ic ultimately owns the information, governments are the custodians.
ey can be exceedingly careful in what they choose to reveal. For more
o decades, federal information commissioners have lamented the state
c access to information in their annual reports, recommending legislanges that would meet the expectations Parliament had intended when i
 he Access to Information Act in 1983. Those recommendations have genllen on the deaf ears of successive governments. In his 2003/04 report,
tion Commissioner John Reid wrote, “For twenty years, Canadians
 information—especially about any subject the government considered
e —have been met by a wall of obstruction, obfuscation and delay.”
 expression of the frustration many journalists and researchers fee
he state of government openness in Canada is the Code of Silence
an ironic tribute awarded each year by the Canadian Association o

ts (CAJ) to what it considers “the most secretive government depart agency in Canada.” The actual award is a plaque featuring a padlock

  Access advocates have proposed a wide array of legislative changes to ma
anadian laws more responsive to the public’s right to know, including maki
l records created for the purposes of administering a publicly funded p
ram subject to access laws, making all exemptions discretionary and limit
nd imposing penalties against departments that withhold records illegally
lease records only after chronic delays. But those proposed solutions h
ad little impact within the halls of governments. While they've earned
ccasional receptive ear from opposition politicians, that receptivity frequen
isappears when those same opposition politicians wind up in government.
  “There's no evidence that these laws create a culture of openness,”
lasdair Roberts. “They provide tools for individuals who want to access in
ation, but you're always fighting against the governmental tendency to w

      Data Journalism

ta journalism has exploded in recent years. Reporters who mas it will find many doors open to them.

oduction

rdiner Expressway is a crucial link for the tens of thousands of com
 who use it daily to drive in and out of Toronto's downtown core. But
g its age, as salt and water intrusion have penetrated and weakened par
concrete structure. Chunks of concrete have fallen onto cars driving
akeshore Boulevard, directly below.
lie Young, a reporter with Global News, decided she wanted to get pa
tine explanations that the problems were merely cosmetic and posed
 to the public. So, she obtained more than 2000 pages of records, throug
om-of-information request, on problems documented by Torontos ci
rs. She then entered the key details into a spreadsheet program.
went through every page of the documents and noted in an Excel she
, severity, location, and details of every incident or inspection that wa
 presenting a danger to the public. I left off the low-severity ones, simp
 there were way too many.”
e work became the basis for a series of stories that showed that deterior
 s more serious than city officials had been saying. She took photograph
 of the ageing highway that were being held up by wooden braces. Th
were presented to readers using an interactive Google Map, and reader
lick on specific locations to see not only a synopsis of each issue, b
e related documents.
 stories won two RTNDA awards and were nominated for a global da
ism award and the CAJ data journalism award.
ung is one of a new generation of data journalists changing the land
f journalism in Canada and around the world, taking data analysi
s pioneered by the first generation of data journalists (who called
er-assisted reporting”), and moving them into the interactive world

```

```
 Data journalism has actually been around since the 1960s, but in the
w years it has gone from being a specialty of a few investigative and b
porters to something that is part of the mainstream. And why not? It mak
ssible a whole new realm of storytelling that involves the audience in a

teractive experience.
 Data journalism is made up of three disciplines that are distinct yet in
pendent. The first is the traditional skills of computer-assisted reporti
ich use data analysis and mapping software to identify newsworthy patte
d trends in data sets that range from a city hall budget to millions of t
g records. The second discipline is data visualization, a technique that is
w but that has been made much easier and accessible with the rise of clo
sed data visualization tools easily available to journalists. The third leg of d
urnalism is custom Web development, using Internet coding skills to cre
ique interactive news applications. Some projects might involve just one
e disciplines, but the most sophisticated might use all three, analyzing data
nd trends, visualizing the results, and creating a unique, customized webs
 share them with the public.
 The tools are many and varied, and range from the simple spreadsheet
mplex computer programming. Any journalists can use the simpler to
ose who put in the time to learn can master the most complex.
 Some of these activities are esoteric, but the use of database and spre
eet programs has become an indispensable part of investigative reporti
d underpins the bulk of our discussion here.

he development of computer-assisted
eporting and data journalism

e techniques that eventually became data journalism were first practised
e United States and advanced rapidly with the arrival of desktop compu
g in the 1980s. Today, data techniques are used in hundreds of newsroo
roughout the United States and are supported by online mailing lists, w
es, university journalism programs, and a unique organization called
tional Institute for Computer-Assisted Reporting, part of the Missouri-ba
oup Investigative Reporters and Editors. The institute runs training cam
thers and sells data to news organizations at cost, and organizes an ann
nference that brings together data journalism practitioners from aro
e world.
 The Canadian evolution of these techniques began in the mid-1990s, wh
handful of curious journalists began to tap into the growing expertise so
 the border. They did stories of remarkable sophistication almost from

```

```
f the efforts. That started to change when the Canadian Association o
 ts (CAJ), which for years had awarded prizes for the best investigativ
 sm in Canada, added a new category for the best story using computer reporting (since renamed the data journalism award).
more recent years, publicity created by the new award, as well as notabl
, such as the Toronto Star’s Race and Crime series on the Toronto
the CBC’s stories on adverse drug reactions, and The Canadian Press’
 n Tasers, have further raised awareness of data work. The technique
 more and more print and broadcast newsrooms, and also by freelan

 bal News established an investigative data desk, which is where Lesli
 orks. At a time when many traditional newsrooms have been layin
s of reporters, some of the few new jobs being created have been fo
rnalists and Web developers. At the same time, the so-called open dat
nt has seen governments across Canada create websites through which
 d more government make data, once only available using freedom-of ion requests, available for free download.
 leading edge of data journalism, as has always been so, is in the United
here a new kind of journalist has evolved: a hybrid reporter-computer
mer. These innovative journalists are using tools adopted from dat
to work with data in ways unimagined just a few years ago.

 Conventional spreadsheet software can do math of just about any kind,
st of the time journalists will stick to sums, percentages, and means.
 Spreadsheets are also good at sorting and filtering information. A sheet
 reordered, or sorted, based on the contents of one or more columns. So,
ample, a spreadsheet of city hall expenditures could be sorted from largest
allest expenditure. The filtering functions of most spreadsheets allow a u
 see only entries that meet certain criteria. For more detailed informati

w.oupcanada.com/Cribb3e.

atabase managers

e newest versions of Excel can handle up to a million rows. (Older versi
ndle up to 65,000 rows.) As soon as you get into extensive data crunchi

ferences instead of the numbers in the cells. The formula A1+B1, for examp
ls the computer to add whatever numbers are in Al and B1.

arching

  is probably the simplest application of data journalism, and differs

 . Both databases and spreadsheets can be used to find information, person’ address from a set of driver's licence records. Database manag
 more powerful, however, in that a user is able to isolate information wi

 When University of King’s College journalism students analyzed a data
 ire calls in the Halifax area in 2014, they were able to search for all m

unting

 n be useful to count the number of times that something happens or
ber of times an entry is found in a database. This technique of analy
 can produce newsworthy information that may contradict conventi
 om.
 Adrian Humphreys, now a reporter with the National Post, shared som

 Humphreys then entered the data into a spreadsheet, and compared
 of impaired charges with the number of cars stopped by the police.
 ced that the number of checks had dropped significantly—in Toronto,
 er had fallen by 66 per cent in five years—and that this decline had
 mentioned by officials who bragged about how the governments poli
 stopped people from drinking and driving. Not surprisingly, the numbe
 ges had dropped dramatically along with the reduced police surveilla

at dubious statistic had been conjured up by police and government—then

orting

is is a method of ranking facts or numbers in order from largest to small
 vice versa. This easily done with campaign donations, or salaries in the c
 Ontario's public-sector employees earning more than $100,000 a year. Th
mes end up on the so-called sunshine list. Under the Public Sector Sal

e names of employees—everyone from judges to firefighters—earning mo
an $100,000 a year. Stories about the highest earners are regularly publis
d broadcast as soon as the government releases the latest numbers the end
e fiscal year, which in Ontario is 31 March. Journalists who use spreadshee

ployers before sorting the salaries, leading to stories about high earners
spitals and school boards, or firefighters, police officers, and nurses earn
x-figures because of overtime.

omparing

omparing entails looking for matches in the information contained in
 more tables. In 2002, The Hamilton Spectator used this technique to mat
e names of donors to Ontario political parties with those of participants
e new, free-market electricity sector created by the Progressive Conservati
vernment. The story revealed that dozens of firms that benefited from
eation of the open market were generous supporters of the PCs.

tting Past the Fear of Numbers

e than a few journalists have been heard to comment that they c
rting as a career because they flunked math and wanted to avoid it
. This aversion often extends to things that are particularly techni
e, as soon as many journalists hear the terms computer-assisted repo
ata journalism, they want to run as fast as they can in the other direct
y fall back on the old notions of what journalism is really about, and dis
 journalism because they are afraid of it. That said, much of this is chan
ata journalism gradually merges with advanced online journalism to c
w hybrid, programmer-reporter, in what some are calling “computati
nalism.”
 There is no question that data work often involves a bit of number cru
sometimes a lot. But the good news is that the math doesn’t get much m

hat Makes a Good Data Investigation?

e answer to this is much the same as the answer to what makes any go
estigation, but there are some special cautions to be exercised when worki
th data. Generally speaking, the more that a database records somethi
t directly affects people, the more likely it is to be the basis for a compelli

estigation.
 Effects on people can be measured in many ways. For example, a databa
adverse drug reactions provides information of acute importance to peopl
ngerous side effects from drugs. Similarly, a database of police arrests appli
intensely personal experiences. Reporters have also used data techniques
estigate meat-packing plants, doctor discipline, vehicular arsons, politi
tronage, pollution, slum landlords, faulty medical devices, immigration, a
l safety. The closer the data gets to people's lives, the more impact your stor
 likely to have.
 Dont make the mistake of believing that just because something
corded in a database, you can miraculously make a story appear from
alyzing boring data will produce a boring story.

teps in the Development of

tabase investigations generally begin in much the same way as more conve

me kind of preliminary legwork that we discussed in Chapter 2.
 Where a database investigation starts to differ is in the process of obtaini
building the data that will help drive the story. Once a story subject has be
ntified, the reporter explores whether there is a public database that mig
ine light on the problem. The need for data may not present itself immed
ly, of course. In some cases, the existence of a database or the likely utility
tabase analysis does not become apparent until later.
 If the matter under investigation is one that falls under some form

th ease. Every time you call a government service number and they look

tailed breakdown of the charges. Compare them with what is allowed in

  Sometimes, agencies will say a portion of the information in the datab

how your reporting is fair and accurate.

atabases as reference sources and tip sheets

ometimes it’s not the big picture that counts, but the fine details.
  While large data investigations often involve, extensive analysis, just sc
ng through a data set can give you story ideas. Databases kept on your h
ive (and updated from time to time) can provide you with quick refere
terial for day-to-day stories.
  Let’s say you analyzed a database of traffic accidents in your communi
 order to find the most dangerous intersections in town. If you keep the d
 hand, you can later check how many accidents have occurred at a particu
cation whenever there is a big accident there. With all the time and effort t
n go into obtaining a data set, squeezing more value out of it in this way
akes sense. Over time, it pays to maintain and update your databases.

ombining Data Journalism
ith Traditional Reporting .

 we said earlier in this chapter, data journalism is an extremely powerful t
t no amount of data analysis will produce a story on its own. To get the sto
u need to combine data with traditional techniques.
  Sometimes, computer analysis will provide rock-solid, irrefutable facts t
n themselves be highlighted in a story. This was the case with The Hamilt
ectator’s series about emissions tests on cars in Ontario. Just as often, analy
ll provide you with good questions to ask. If you determine an intersect
s more accidents than any other, you still have work to do to find out w
hat are the traffic volumes along that section of roadway, and what effect
ey have on accidents? What physical features might obstruct views and l
 accidents? Is there a problem with the traffic signals?
  To answer such questions, you may need to interview traffic officials, at
nt victims, pedestrians, and local residents, as well as find documents, s
 detailed accident reports, and so on. The data can point you to the story,
ly reporting will land it. This is why it is called computer-assisted reporti

d not reporter-assisted computing.

 Narrative journalism—using storytelling techniques to engage,

ntroduction

rrative journalism—using storytelling techniques to engage, explain, a
luminate—brings together the immediacy of journalism and the allure
her narrative writing to produce extraordinary stories.
 Your hard drive is brimming with PDF documents. Interviews with the
gures are complete and transcribed. The research phase of the investigat

at has the investigation uncovered? Will the story make damaging alle

mes B. Stewart.' The writer's task is to convey the information gleaned fr
untless sources in a coherent way, while at the same time catching and ho
g the attention of the reader, viewer, or listener. In short, the writer must
e story truthfully and effectively.

  It is pointless to begin writing until you are locked and loaded on yo

rge ahead regardless.

 Narrative journalism—using storytelling techniques to engage, expla

ers interesting elements along the way.
 “Show, don't tell” is an often-repeated rule of narrative writing—and
od reason. It is not enough to say that child poverty is endemic and grow
rse, according to statistics and public records. Such factual and statist

ryteller, your job is to appeal to the heart. You need to combine the simp

e course of your story and you will earn the trust of your audience. Str
rrative writing reinforced by reputable evidence builds a gradual sense

```
he information has to feel authentic. Too often, journalists lose the fait
r audience by being too simplistic. Black-and-white truths are rare; most
ant human endeavours are rendered in shades of grey. Stories that reduce
cated issues to good-versus-evil scenarios risk losing their sense of auth
y. Always present the grey areas in your story with the knowledge tha
complexities only enrich the authenticity of what you write. A person,
ny, or government department portrayed as a one-dimensional villain
s interesting than a fully realized, complex subject.
oy Peter Clark, a celebrated writing coach and senior scholar with th
a-based Poynter Institute, has done a great deal of analysis of tools fo
istic narrative writing and recommends a number of well-tested, basi
g techniques for bringing sleepy prose to life. Among them:

  Use verbs in the strongest form, the simple present or past. Stron

  verbs create action, save words, and reveal the players.
  Dig for the concrete and specific: the name of the dog and the brand o
  the beer. Seek details that appeal to the senses.
  Prefer the simple over the technical: shorter words, sentences, and
  paragraphs at the points of greatest complexity.
  Control the pace of the story by varying sentence and paragraph
  length.

lark also points to a number of narrative techniques that can apply t
gative writing. For example, one of his tools urges writers to “use interna
ngers to move readers to turn the page.’? Consider the stunning openragraphs to a series of news stories written by US journalist Anne Hull

 When you do have someone to illustrate your story, be sure not to give

el-Proofing the Story

vestigative story can be hard-hitting. It may accuse a politician of conflic
 est or a company of cheating customers or polluting the environment.
 lege a crime or other wrongdoing has been committed, and it may nam
alleged to be responsible. Most investigative stories published or broad

 vestigative reporters must ensure the information they report is accurat
 mplete. Allegations must be based on verifiable facts and come fro

etween differing points of view. And they must make every effort to off
 ccused of wrongdoing a chance to refute the allegations against them.
urnalists should view the law of defamation not as a barrier to report

er they liked about whomever they liked, any notion of media cred
would vanish. Rumours, hearsay, the conclusions of a reporter’s cursor
 ation, the ravings of every conspiracy theorist who walks through th
 om door—all would be fair game. “If no one had any redress for libel

 f individuals, it also helps to ensure that the information reaching th

 alists conduct themselves professionally and responsibly, the chance

  This section offers an overview of the law of defamation and the st
vestigative reporters can take to ensure their stories will withstand legal sc
 y. If the story is controversial or puts someone's reputation into questio
e media organization's lawyers undoubtedly will be called in to vet the pi
d offer advice on how to handle passages that may be vulnerable to a lawsu

nimize the legal risk of running the story.

nderstanding defamation

ny statement that is made to others by any means—published in a newspap
 magazine, broadcast on radio or television, posted to a website, or circula
a social media—could be considered defamatory if it damages the repu
on of a specific person. The classic definition of a libel is any statement t
nds to discredit or lower an individual “in the estimation of right-thinki
embers of society generally.” The same goes for a statement that may ca
hers to shun or avoid a person, or tends to expose the person to “hatr
dicule, or contempt.” Put another way, a false statement about someone, ma
 that person’s discredit, would be defamatory.'? Law professor Robert Mart
s suggested a more down-to-earth definition of the kinds of statements t
ould be a warning flag for journalists: “A libel? he says, “is simply somethi
u would not like to see said in public about yourself?

ho can sue for libel?
e law of defamation is concerned with protecting the reputations of id
fiable individuals. For a claim to succeed, the person suing—known a
aintiff—must establish that he or she is the person portrayed in a bad ligh
media report.'* Corporations are considered “persons” under Canadian
d have the right to sue to protect their reputations. Courts have ruled t
corporated entities, such as unions and non-profit groups, can sue for li
ile the right to sue does not extend to a body without legal status, such
 ad-hoc citizen's committee, individuals belonging to this sort of group co
ing an action if they could show that a statement is related to their perso
nduct. In the case of a company, the allegation must relate to the way it c
 the development of the new libel defence of responsible communication
tters of public interest shows, the courts increasingly expect the conduct
urnalists and media organizations to be consistent with the ethical standar
 the profession.

 The defence is based on the “responsible journalism” defence developed
 British courts.*? The Ontario courts implemented it in 2007 and 2008,* a
 Supreme Court's final rulings have added refinements that now apply acro
 country. The Court has taken many of the elements of professional, ethic
porting—being fair, using solid sources, chasing important stories—and r
nized them in law.
 Here's how the defence works: First, a media report containing a potenti
el must address an issue of public interest. This “is not synonymous wi
at interests the public,’ the Supreme Court cautioned, and would not inclu
ere curiosity or prurient interest” in the private lives of public figures
ebrities. Second, the issue need not be of national importance or of intere
a wide audience. A battle over development in a rural area may be of lo
erest, for instance, but involves wider issues of land use and environment
icy. In the Court's words, “it is enough that some segment of the communit
uld have a genuine interest in receiving information on the subject.” Thi
ries can be about government, politics, or subjects “ranging from scien
d the arts to the environment, religion, and morality.” The media repor
t led to the creation of the defence offer good examples. One was an Otta
izen story that alleged an Ontario police officer had hampered rescue effo
Ground Zero after the 11 September 2001 terrorist attacks. In the other,
ronto Star reported allegations that an Ontario businessman's political co
tions helped win approval for a controversial golf-course expansion.
how the story was reported. A judge or jury will assess whether journa
r and the Ottawa Citizen.*° In essence, this defence gives the media the rig
be wrong. It prevents libel claims from succeeding against news stories
portant public issues, even if they include errors or statements that damag
meone's reputation, as long as journalists made reasonable efforts to confi

preme Court decision shifts the focus from what was published or broadca

 acted responsibly and, despite their best efforts, some facts or allegatio
rned out to be wrong or false. This reflects the legal test applied to doctor
yers, and other professionals facing allegations of negligence. Journalis

erating table, but not every death is the result of malpractice. The issuew the operation was conducted and whether the surgeon's actions were re
able in the circumstances. “Productive debate is dependent on the free fl

ed on the British responsible journalism defence) to consider:

« The seriousness of the allegation: The more serious and damaging
  allegation, the more diligent the journalist must be in une
  verifying the story.
¢ The public importance of the matter: Not all subjects of public inte
  have the same weight or importance. Stories exploring “grave matt
  of national security,’ the Court said, will require more diligence
  investigate than those on “the prosaic business of everyday politics.
¢ The urgency of the matter: The courts will defer to the need to
  timely reports on important allegations and events, but mistakes m
   in the rush to score a scoop might not be forgiven if a reasonable d

  have an ax to grind, a reporter will be expected to show extra dilige

  defence, and it may be the most significant facet of the ruling
  recognizes that the public may have an interest in the allegations
  countercharges made in debates over important issues, regardless
  whether these allegations are true. While the law punishes those w
  repeat a libel, in this context, the messenger would have a defence

  (a) the statement is attributed, “preferably” to a named source;
  (b) the news report indicates the statement has not been verified;
  (c) both sides of the dispute are reported fairly; and
  (d) the report sets out the context in which the statement was made

```

```
 This defence does not give journalists a licence to destroy reputations.
s list makes clear, the courts will demand high standards of conduct and
fence will be of little or no use to reporters who are sloppy or fail to keep
en mind as they do their stories. The journalist's actions and methods a
 news organization's code of conduct will be put under the microscope a
asured against the accepted ethical standards of journalism. But the defen
courages investigative journalism. It lessens libel chill and modernizes la
t, for too long, have put reputations ahead of society's need for hard-hitti
urnalism on important public issues. ;
 One other important point: The Supreme Court deliberately named t
 defence of “responsible communication” because the definition of the te
rnalist is expanding in our multimedia age. Anyone “publishing materi
public interest in any medium’—bloggers and non-journalists included—
vered. Established journalistic practices will be used to assess the conduct
ournalists and non-journalists alike,’ the Court said, and standards will evol
 keep pace with the norms of new communications media.” Defamati
w and its defences already apply to publication on the Internet, but this
nificant development. The ruling may encourage more high-quality, publi
erest reporting by bloggers and citizen journalists.
 The new defence has come before the courts in several cases, offer
ights into how judges and juries will apply it to a journalist's work. In 20
 independent local news site in Sault Ste. Marie, Ontario, relied on
fence when a man with a criminal record for fraud, who was promoting n
siness ventures, sued for defamation over a news report. SooToday.com h
ed a fraud alert issued by police, offered an independent analysis of the bu
s plan, consulted more than 20 sources and tried to locate the businessm
 comment. Based on this thorough research, a jury accepted the responsib
mmunication defence and dismissed the action. In other cases, the defen
s failed because journalists relied on biased sources or second-hand inf
tion, reported allegations as fact, or did not make sufficient efforts to cont
e target of the story. The use of sensational or inflammatory words, witho
pporting evidence, has also defeated the defence—in one case, the headli
                                                                   .
  « Any other relevant circumstances: A catch-all provision for oth
   aspects of the story, including the tone of the article. Sensationali
    or a critical tone may be appropriate, the Court noted, and write
   should not be held to “a standard of stylistic blandness.
   investigative reporting often takes a trenchant or adversarial positi
   on pressing issues of the day.”

Here's a hypothetical example of how the defence could work: A journal
tigates an accusation that a restaurant inspector has taken bribes in retu
gnoring food-safety violations. Police confirm they are looking into
ation, but refuse to identify the inspector. Two city hall sources confi
ame, and say he has been suspended. The mayor calls a news conference

d tainted food. The reporter calls the inspector's office and home five tim
ng comment, to no avail. The story, naming the inspector, runs on

The allegation turns out to be false. No charges are laid, the inspector
tated, and public safety was never at risk. The inspector’s reputation clea
een damaged, but would the responsible communication defence defea
action? The story would meet the following criteria:

  The bribery allegation is clearly a matter of public interest.
  The sources were solid.
  The reporter made reasonable efforts to verify the information.
  The bribery allegation had some substance and was under poli
  investigation.
  There was urgency in publishing—people’s health might be at risk.
  Efforts were made to interview the inspector.
  The story was not sensationalized and allegations were not report
  as fact.

story came up short on two fronts. First, it did not reflect the inspec
iew of events, but the journalist’s efforts to contact him would offset t
coming. And, second, the seriousness of the false allegation compounde
amage to the inspector's reputation. But the story was of intense publ
tance and the reporting was thorough and solid. The newspaper shou
le to claim the defence of responsible communication.

ch information. Teachers, for instance, have a legal duty to report suspect

alicious reporting

urnalists must act professionally, investigate thoroughly, double-check fa
nd strive to produce a story that is accurate, balanced, and fair. As noted,
fences of fair comment, qualified privilege, and responsible journalism
il if a story was published or broadcast maliciously—if it is clear the journal
 media outlet was motivated by spite, acted in bad faith, or was “out to g
omeone. While motivation is difficult to prove, the courts have said that
ence of malice can include a sensational treatment of the news, exaggerat
 distorting facts, or using words that are unnecessarily harsh. Journalists h
en judged to have acted with malice for failing to contact the target of
ory, not bothering to check whether information is accurate, leaving out
cts, taking statements out of context, or publishing information known to
lse.*° Evidence of malice may come directly from those who produced
ory. In one libel case, where a serious allegation of political corruption tur

t to be a fabrication, an editor had referred to the plaintiff as a “sleaze”
e reporter had boasted of catching the plaintiff red-handed.”

 journalism: The best defence

journalism—reporting done professionally, accurately, and fairly—is t
efence against the threat of a libel suit. Here are some tips on how to avo
sued.

e-check facts against previous news stories, official reports, and any oth

ents that corroborate a controversial statement or assertion. Seek out
ne independent source to back up what the main source of the story sa
ed. Look for documents covered by qualified privilege—such as cou
s and transcripts of debates—that support the allegations or a source
n of events. Journalists scrambling to make deadline or to avoid bei
d may feel pressured into rushing stories into print or onto the air befo
ave been fully researched and verified. Many costly defamation cas
have been avoided if reporters [and ] [editors ] [had ] [taken ] [more ] [time ] [to ] [g]

 The Registry of Motor Vehicles was unable to adequately justify t
 public policy; it could neither explain why the most recent changes we
 warranted nor prove that the inspections were accomplishing their g
 of keeping the streets safer. The province still hasn't explained or de
 onstrated why inspections are necessary in Nova Scotia and not in ot
 jurisdictions.

ills Exercises

 To find out how much various garages would charge to repair a car, Jeff
 Simpson did not identify himself as a journalist until after receiving
 estimate. Was this ethical? What are the advantages and disadvantag
 of this approach? What other methods could he have used to investig
 this story?
 Suggest other consumer or customer service concerns or problems t
 could be investigated in this way, and discuss how you would gather inf
 mation and present the story.

spend or give back to investors, which is a go
indication that things are going well.

chief executive officer (CEO) the person in
charge of the company, and the conduit betwee
the board of directors and the company.

chief financial officer (CFO) the executive in
charge of the company’s finances.

commodity a physical substance such as a
good, grain, or metal that may be traded for
cash or another substance.

credit In one sense, credit is just another word
for debt. Credit is given to customers when
they are allowed to make a purchase with the
promise to pay later, as with a consumer credi
card. A bank gives credit when it lends money.

creditor one to whom a debt is owed; in
insolvency matters, a creditor is an individual
having a claim provable under the Bankruptcy
and Insolvency Act.’

current assets a balance sheet item that
equals the sum of cash (and cash equivalents),
accounts receivable, inventory, marketable
securities, prepaid expenses, and other assets
that could be converted to cash in less than
one year. A company’s creditors will often be
interested in how much that company has
in current assets, since these can easily be
liquidated if the company goes bankrupt. In
addition, current assets are important to most
companies as a source of funds for day-to-day

```

```

total of what the company owes and must
within a year. In order to determine the
any’s real financial health, the reporter
ld compare the current debt to the current
s. The company is in trouble if it owes
 money than it has to spend.

or one who owes money to another.’

eciation the allocation of the cost of an

property due to general wear and tear or
lescence.!”

ty the difference between what a property

e and the remaining mortgage or loan

loss a reduction in the value of an investment
or a condition in which a company’s expenses
exceed its revenues. It is the opposite of profit

material news (or material information) an
event that could affect the value of a share. A
company is legally obliged to file information
about such changes with the relevant securiti
regulator.

net income a company’s total earnings after
paying the costs of doing business, depreciation, interest, taxes, and other expenses. It
is the same as net profit, and represents the .
proverbial bottom line. Do not confuse net
income with operating income.'*

Ontario Securities Commission (OSC) the
largest Canadian securities commission. It
is a self-funded Crown corporation, with a
mandate to provide protection to investors
from unfair, improper, or fraudulent practice
and to foster fair and efficient capital markets
in Ontario and confidence in their integrity.

operating income the amount of money a
company earns before deductions for interest
payments and income taxes. It’s also known
operating profit. Unlike the net income, it do
not represent the bottom line.

price-to-earnings ratio a basic measure
investors use to evaluate a company. To figur
out the P-E ratio, divide the stock price by t
earnings per share (EPS).”°

private company a company that does not
trade its shares on a public stock exchange.
(Shares can be traded privately—for exampl
among a group of employees.)

public company a company whose shares
are traded on a public stock exchange, and
which is subject to the rules of securities
regulators, such as the Securities and Exchan
Commission (SEC) in the United States or th
Ontario Securities Commission. Unlike priv
companies, a public company must disclose
its financial information to the commission
question, which in turn makes it available to

’s financial performance. To get the

ter taxes, divided by sales for a given

that is backed by collateral .?° Secured creditor
receive priority during the bankruptcy
process.

security an investment instrument (or document) that offers evidence of a debt or equity.
is usually a stock (which is a form of equity) o
bond (which relates to debt). ‘

shareholder a holder of stock who has a claim
on a part of a corporation's assets and earnings
In other words, a shareholder is an owner of
a company. Ownership is determined by the
number of shares a person owns relative to th
number of outstanding shares (or the stock
owned by other investors).”’ For example, if
a company has a thousand shares of stock
outstanding, and one person owns a hundred
shares, he or she would own 10 per cent of the
company’s assets.

unsecured creditor a person or company owe
debt, such as credit cards, medical bills, and
utility bills, that are not backed by collateral.
Unsecured creditors are among the last to get
their money—if at all—during the bankruptcy
process.”®

stock a type of security that signifies ownershi
in a corporation and represents a claim on
part of the outfit’s assets and earnings. Stocks
are the foundation of nearly every investment
portfolio, and they have historically outperformed other investments over the long run.
There are two main types of stock: common an
preferred. Common stock usually entitles the
owner to the right to vote at shareholder meetings and to receive dividends that the company
has declared. Preferred stock generally does no
garner voting rights, but has a higher claim on
assets and earnings than the common shares.
For example, owners of preferred stock receive
dividends before common shareholders and
have priority if the company goes bankrupt
and is liquidated.

trustee in bankruptcy a person licensed by th
superintendent of bankruptcy to administer a
bankruptcy or proposal.”

ecurities Regulators

 nada

nada does not have a national securities regulator. Instead, we have pro
ial regulators. There is talk of replacing the provincial regulators with
 ional body. The Ontario Securities Commission (OSC) is by far the larg
d tends to deal with the major Canadian companies (which is why we foc
 the OSC in this book). The other Canadian regulators require much the sa
formation as the OSC does. Websites for the major regulators are listed here

  e Alberta: www.albertasecurities.com
  ¢ British Columbia: www.bcsc.bc.ca
  e Ontario: www.osc.gov.on.ca
  ¢ Quebec: www.lautorite.qc.ca/en/index.html

e documents held by all these regulators can be searched using a speci
earinghouse called SEDAR (www.sedar.com), which also contains links to pr
 s of each public company that trades stock in a given province.

ited States

e national regulator is the Securities and Exchange Commission (SEC): www
 .gov. The site has a limited search engine called Edgar. A more useful
pensive option is called Live Edgar.
  State securities regulators can, and have, acted independently of the SEC,
 worth checking their sites if you're conducting a serious investigation int
mpany that does business in the United States. See the website of the Nor
erican Securities Administrators Association, at www.nasaa.org.

 perations minus property and equipment purchases.

g or whether it’s having trouble collecting from its customers. If the deb
 asing, is the company earning enough money to pay it off?

nalysts as Journalistic Sources

 ge Canadian and US companies are followed and monitored by analys
o work for brokerage houses, such as CIBC World Markets. In general term
se brokerage houses buy stock for clients and manage their portfolios. I
 analyst’s job to prepare reports for investors, who are clients of the broke
 house, on whether certain shares or bonds are worth buying. Most analys
o work at brokerage houses have beats such as transportation, or oil and g
 lysts can tell you how well the industrial sector or even a specific compan
doing. Depending on the analyst’s viewpoint, the information may not
 irely balanced. So the reporter must be wary.
 “You always have to be careful,” warns Christopher Waddell, a professor
 rnalism at Carleton University.
 Assume that people don’t tell you something for nothing. They
u something for a reason and you've got to figure out what the reasonbsequent to the Enrons and [other corporate scandals], there is now a muc
 ater emphasis on disclosing when there are potential conflicts of inter
tween the analyst's employer and the company the analyst is talking abou
d that also includes the analyst, too. You certainly find on an analyst's repo
disclaimer page at the end, which will say that CIBC World Markets ow
 res in this company, or acts as an investment advisor for this company. A
 analyst may also own shares.
 Analysts also work for other institutions that invest money, such as
tario Teachers’ Pension Plan. It is their job to examine the market, the eco
y, and the performance of certain companies to determine where the p
ould be investing its money. However, Waddell points out that the analy
o tend to be quoted the most frequently in the mainstream media are tho

```

```
o get a sense of which analysts may be the best sources for balanced infor
, it is best to do what is done with any source: phone around and as
ons. It’s worth asking some of a company’s biggest investors, who ar
in its proxy filing. You can also contact a securities firm’s public-relation
tment and acquire contact information for analysts, as well as informa
bout the stocks or bonds they cover. Erik Schatzker of Bloomberg warn
many analysts have been prohibited from talking to reporters or have
earance from PR.”
ou can also check out debt-rating agencies, which evaluate the credit
ness of a company. Schatzker says that “the reports they produce ca
re sober and less cheerleading than what some equity analysts write
ubject matter is, however, complex and the language arcane.” The tw
t such agencies are Standard & Poor's (www.standardandpoors.com) an

’s Investors Service (www.moodys.com).*4

Lexis
www.lexis.com
A searchable database of case law and congressional actions. Thoug
costly, many news organizations have a corporate account.

Pacer
www.pacer.gov
Pacer is a US courts service for electronic case and docket informatio
from federal, appellate, state, and district courts, as well as bankruptc
courts. It is not as expensive as Lexis. Through his pursuit of compan
ies that made drugs and other products, former New York attorn
general (and more recently disgraced governor) Eliot Spitzer showe
that investigations carried out at the US state level can be good source
of information about companies and executives.

United States Department of Justice
www.justice. gov
According to its website, the department has become “the world’s la
gest law office and the central agency for enforcement of federal laws

Bankruptcy court documents
www.uscourts.gov/bankruptcycourts. html
This is the general site. You may need to find out the particular “circui
where a case in question is located. If you know where a bankruptc
hearing might be taking place, you can follow the appropriate link
Incidentally, you could also visit Bankruptcydata.com, which main
offers a service that costs money but can also be used as a handy refe
ence site. Plug in the name of a company you suspect might be goi
bankrupt—this may produce information showing that documents
exist and where they are located.

Courtlink
www. lexisnexis.com/courtlink/online
This is a Nexis product that charges a fee but offers some free servic
such as the latest news about companies.

Findlaw
www.findlaw.com
This is a very useful free online service. The search engine allows y
to hunt for information on US government sites, plus others. It al

of the public,” he wrote. Media law expert

name a confidential source or to produce
records that would identify one. This
right would not be absolute—a judge
could order disclosure if it was in the
public interest to identify the source, if
all other means of identifying the source
had been exhausted, and after considerin
the impact on the source. Journalists
would also have the right to withhold
unpublished information unless it was
deemed to be of vital importance and
could not be obtained by other means.
Also, the legislation would require a judg
or justice of the peace to be satisfied,
before issuing a warrant to search a
journalist’s materials or a media outlet,
that the public interest in investigating an
prosecuting an offence takes precedence
over a journalist's right to gather and
report information.
At least three have been jailed since
1900. In 1914, Nova Scotia's legislature
jailed William McCurdy, news editor
of the Halifax Herald, for 48 hours for
refusing to identify the author of a letter
to the editor that accused politicians of
corruption. In 1969, when FLQ bombings
in Quebec culminated in the October
Crisis, Montreal’s fire commissioner jaile
a CBC Television reporter for seven days
for refusing to name a man who claimed
to be a bomber. In the third case, a camera
operator with a television station in Hull,
Quebec, spent 72 hours in jail in 1995
but later lost his job after acknowledging
he had never promised to protect the
source’s identity. See William March,

```

```

protecting source” (The Globe and Mail, 29
Sept. 1995).

David McKie, “Bird strikes increasing at
Canadian airports” (CBC News, 14 May

plan that spells out the steps the airport is
taking to keep birds and wildlife away fro
planes. Neither Transport Canada nor the
airport in question will advertise these
plans, so you must demand them—either
informally with a request to a mediarelations person, or formally through an
access-to-information request.

that is illegal in Canada—their advertising
practices are under constant, if not always
rigorous, scrutiny. The FDA has an online
database (www.fda.gov/foi/warning.htm)
of warning letters. During the publicity
surrounding anti-inflammatory drugs,
these warning letters revealed that the
companies had been told about misleading
advertising, which came in handy for
reporters doing stories in Canada.

 is a book they should own and keep on their desk wherever they go.”

 ... like the focus on investigative reporting—both simple and highly technical—

 as well as the Canadian laws and regulations that students need to know.”

                      —Gillian Steward, Mount Royal University

 ow it its third successful edition, Digging Deeper offers Canadian journa

 students the tools they need to enter the fast-paced world of investigative repor

 en by a team of award-winning journalists, this comprehensive guide outlines

 evelop story ideas, conduct research, pitch stories, and turn raw information

 elling investigative reports for print, radio, television, and the Web. Digging De

 indispensable resource that will provide valuable guidance to students throug

 education and their careers.

 hlights

 anadian examples and case studies, many new to the third edition, make th

 esearching and reporting process highly accessible to Canadian students.

 focus on independent thinking shows students how to escape the “spin cy

 y deciding for themselves what is newsworthy and finding their own sources
 nformation.

 fully revised chapter on data journalism offers students a complete

 nderstanding of this increasingly important area of investigative journalism.

 ew exercises and discussion questions give students the opportunity to pra

 nd apply the techniques they have learned in each chapter.

 nalism at the Ryerson School of Journalism. DEAN JOBB, a former reporter, e
